Conditions for a Qadi in Shariah
Listing conditions for a qadi with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required for a qadi in Hanafi law and identify which situations make a qadi's ruling valid despite an invalid appointment.

Core Idea
A qadi must be Muslim, mukallaf, sound-sensed, knowledgeable, and not bribed; but a faulty appointment doesn't always void a correct ruling.

In this passage, Mawlana Ashraf Ali Thanwi outlines the essential conditions for a shariah judge (qadi) based on Hanafi fiqh. He distinguishes between conditions of appointment and validity of rulings, showing that a deficiently appointed qadi may still have his judgments enforced if they conform to shariah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general conditions for a qadi
Read the passage and list all the conditions mentioned: being Muslim, mukallaf (adult and sane), having sound senses (sight, hearing, speech), not obtaining the position through bribery, and possessing religious knowledge.
These are conditions for a valid appointment; lack of any makes the appointment invalid.
2
Understand the knowledge condition and its exception
Note that an ignorant (jahil) qadi cannot be appointed. However, if an ignorant person becomes qadi and rules correctly by seeking a fatwa from scholars, the ruling is legally effective.
The appointment itself remains invalid, but the ruling stands if it matches shariah.
3
Analyze the uprightness (fasiq) condition
A fasiq (open sinner) cannot be appointed as qadi, even if he is a scholar. If he does become qadi and rules correctly, the ruling is effective, but the Muslim community must work to remove him from office.
This balances the validity of the judgment with the obligation to uphold integrity.
4
Distinguish between appointment validity and ruling validity
The passage shows that appointment requires strict conditions, but a ruling can be valid even if the appointment is defective, provided the ruling itself is correct according to shariah.
Key distinction for understanding judicial authority in Hanafi law.
5
Apply the conditions to hypothetical scenarios
Consider cases where one condition is missing (e.g., blind, bribed, non-Muslim). Determine whether the appointment is invalid and whether the ruling would still be effective if correct.
Practice reinforces the nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاضی کے لیے شرعاً جو صفات ضروری نہیں اُن کی تفصیل کتب فقہ ہدایہ ، عالمگیری رد المختار وغیرہ میں موجود ہے بوقت ضرورت مراجعت کر لی جائے اس جگہ چند ضروری صفات کو بیان کیا جاتا ہے۔ شرعی قاضی کی شرائط قاضی شرعی کے لیے شرط یہ ہے کہ قاضی مسلمان ہو غیر مسلم قاضی نہیں ہوں۔ نہیں ہو سکتا ، اسی طریقہ سے قاضی کا مکلف ہونا بھی شرط ہے بچہ یا مجنون قاضی نہیں بن سکتے ، یہ بھی شرط ہے کہ آنکھ کان، زبان صحیح و سالم ہوں اندھا یا بہرا یا گونگا نہ ہو اور قاضی ہونے کے لیے یہ بھی شرط ہے کہ رشوت دے کر قاضی نہ بنا ہو اور اگر رشوت دے کر قاضی بنا ہو تو وہ شرعاً قاضی نہ ہوگا اور اس کا حکم بھی نافذ نہ ہو گا (کذا فی البحر ) اور یہ بھی ضروری ہے کہ علم دین میں مہارت تامہ رکھتا ہو جاہل کو قاضی بنانا جائز نہیں۔ یہ الگ بات ہے کہ اگر کسی جگہ جاہل قاضی بن گیا اور اس نے اہل علم سے فتوی لے کر شریعیت کے موافق فیصلہ کر دیا تو اس کا حکم ماخذ ہو جائے گا ۔ اسی طریقہ سے فاسق کو بھی قاضی بنانا جائز نہیں۔ اگر چہ وہ عالم بھی ہو اگر چہ اُس کا فیصلہ نافذ ہو جائے گا بشرطیکہ شریعت کے موافق ہو لیکن مسلمانوں پر واجب ہو گا کہ اس کے معزول کرانے کی کوشش کریں۔
The qualities that are not necessary for a qadi according to shariah — their details are present in fiqh books like Hidayah, Alamgiri, Radd al-Mukhtar etc. When needed, refer to them. Here a few necessary qualities are mentioned. Conditions for a shariah qadi: It is a condition that the qadi be Muslim; a non-Muslim cannot be a qadi. Similarly, it is a condition that the qadi be obliged (mukallaf); a child or insane person cannot become a qadi. It is also a condition that his eyes, ears, and tongue be sound; he should not be blind, deaf, or mute. And it is also a condition that he should not become a qadi by giving a bribe; if he became a qadi by bribe, then he is not a qadi according to shariah, and his judgment will not be effective (kadha fi al-Bahr). And it is also necessary that he possesses complete expertise in religious knowledge; making an ignorant person a qadi is not permissible. However, if an ignorant person became a qadi in some place and he gave a ruling in accordance with shariah by taking a fatwa from scholars, then his ruling will be considered effective (makhidh). Similarly, a fasiq (transgressor) cannot be made a qadi, even if he is a scholar, although his ruling will be effective if it conforms to shariah, but it is obligatory upon Muslims to strive to have him removed.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage lists the essential conditions for a valid qadi in Islamic law. The primary conditions include: being Muslim, being mukallaf (adult and sane), having intact senses (vision, hearing, speech), not having obtained the position through bribery, and possessing sufficient religious knowledge. There are exceptions: an ignorant person who becomes a qadi but rules correctly by seeking guidance from scholars has his ruling valid, though his appointment is not valid. Similarly, a fasiq (open sinner) cannot be appointed as qadi, but if he does rule correctly, the ruling stands; however, the community must work to remove him from office. These conditions ensure the integrity of the judiciary.

Key Terms
قاضی (qadi): Islamic judge who resolves disputes according to shariah
مکلف (mukallaf): Legally responsible person (adult and sane)
رشوت (rishwat): Bribe; obtaining position through bribe invalidates the appointment
فاسق (fasiq): Open transgressor, sinner

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which condition, if violated, makes both the appointment AND the ruling void?
A) Being Muslim
B) Being a scholar of fiqh
C) Not being appointed by bribe
D) Having sound senses

Key Takeaway
A qadi's appointment requires strict conditions, but a correct ruling can be effective even from a deficiently appointed qadi, except when bribery is involved.

The Condition of Severe Necessity for Adopting Another Madhhab
Defining the condition for adopting another madhhab with ijma evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for giving fatwa according to another madhhab and apply it to distinguish valid necessity from personal desire.

Core Idea
Adopting another madhhab is only permitted under severe necessity, not personal whim—by consensus.

This passage sets forth a fundamental rule for muftis: when considering a ruling from a different school, the shift must be driven by genuine necessity, not personal convenience. The author emphasizes that this condition is established by ijma (consensus) and provides concrete examples from Ibn Taymiyyah to illustrate improper switching. We will unpack the condition, its evidentiary basis, and how to apply it in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stated condition
The passage clearly states that adopting another madhhab must be due to severe necessity (darurah shadidah), not for personal desire (khwahish nafsani). This is the primary condition.
The condition is absolute and applies to all cases of switching madhhabs.
2
Recognize the evidentiary basis
The author cites Allamah Ibn Taymiyyah who transmitted that this condition is established by ijma (consensus of the ummah). In Hanafi usul, ijma is a binding source of law (qat'i al-thubut).
Consensus makes the rule authoritative and not open to reinterpretation.
3
Distinguish necessity from desire
The examples from the passage illustrate improper motivation: a man who married with fasiq witnesses (valid in Hanafi) later claims the marriage was invalid in Shafi'i to escape the triple divorce consequence. This is switching for convenience, not necessity.
Necessity implies a genuine hardship or compelling circumstance, not a preferred outcome.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Consider a Hanafi mufti asked about a woman who wants to adopt Shafi'i inheritance law to receive a share. Since her desire for a larger share is not a severe necessity, the condition is not met, and she must follow her own school.
The mufti must assess the mustafti's motive and the degree of hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دوسرے مذہب پر فتوی دینے کی مد د شراکیا و تفصیلات اس سلسلہ میں پہلی شرط یہ ہے کہ مذہب غیر ریل کرنا ضرورت شدیدہ کی وجہ سے ہو، خواہش نفسانی کے لیے نہ ہو اور اس شرط پر تمام امت کا اجماع اور اتفاق علامہ ابن تیمیہ نے نقل کیا ہے : حيث قال في من نكاح عند شهود فسقة شو طلقها ثلانافاراد ها التخلص من العدمة المغلظة بأن النكاح كان ناسدا في الأصل على مذهب الشافعي فلم يقع الطلاق انقه وهذا القول بحالت اجماع المسلمين سوار فانهم متفقون على انام اعتقد حل الشيئ كان عليه ان يعتقد ذالك سواء وافق عرضه او خالف ومن اعتقد تحريمد كان عليه ان يعتقد ذالك في الحالين وهولاء المطلقون الايفكرون في واد النظام يفسق الولى الأمد الطلاق الثلاث لا عند الاستمتاء والتوارث يكونون في وقت يقلدون من يفد وو في وقت يقلدون من يصححه بحب الفين والهوى و مثل هذا لا يجرز با تفاق الامه وثم قال بعد ثلاثة أسطر ونظير هذا ان يعتقد الرجل بثوت مستفعة الجواب اذا كانا طالباً لها وعدم ثبوتها اذا كان مشتريا فان هذا لا يخبر زبالاجماع وكذا من صحة ولايت الفاسق في حال نكاحة وبنى على فساد ولایت حال طلاقه لم يجز ذالك باجماع المسلمين ولوقال المستفتي المعين انا لم اكن اعرف
On giving fatwa according to another madhhab: complete conditions and details. In this matter, the first condition is that adopting another madhhab must be due to severe necessity, not for personal desire. There is consensus of the entire ummah on this condition, as transmitted by Allamah Ibn Taymiyyah, where he said: '... about a man who married with fasiq witnesses, then divorced her three times, and wanted to avoid the severe consequence by claiming that the marriage was originally invalid in the Shafi'i school, so the divorce did not occur. And this statement is with the consensus of the Muslims, except that they agree that if a person believes something to be permissible, he must act accordingly, whether it agrees with his desire or not; and if he believes something to be forbidden, he must act accordingly in both cases. And those who divorce, they do not consider the sequence, they become fasiq. ... And similar to this is a person who believes in the validity of a benefit if he is the claimant and denies it if he is the purchaser; this is not permissible by consensus. Similarly, one who believes in the validity of a fasiq's guardianship in marriage and then based on its invalidity when divorcing, this is not permissible by consensus. And if the specific mustafti says: 'I did not know...'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage sets forth the critical condition for giving a fatwa according to a madhhab other than one's own: it must be due to severe necessity, not out of personal whim. This is a matter of consensus. Ibn Taymiyyah is quoted to illustrate with examples: a man who married with fasiq witnesses and then divorced his wife three times, seeking to avoid the consequence of triple divorce by claiming the marriage was originally invalid in the Shafi'i school, so the divorce did not occur. This is not permissible because it is switching madhhabs for convenience. Similarly, one cannot adopt contradictory positions based on what benefits him in a given situation. The principle is that once a person believes something is permissible or forbidden, he must remain consistent regardless of personal interest. Talfiq (picking and choosing) for selfish reasons is unanimously prohibited.

Key Terms
مذہب غیر (madhhab ghayr): A different school of Islamic law
ضرورت شدیدہ (darurah shadidah): Severe necessity that warrants departure from the normative ruling
اجماع (ijma): Consensus of scholars, a source of Islamic law
تلفیق (talfiq): Combining rulings from different madhhabs to achieve a desired outcome

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for giving a fatwa according to a different madhhab according to this passage?
A) It must be easier and more convenient.
B) It must be due to severe necessity, not personal desire.
C) It must be a popular opinion.
D) It must be from the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
Adopting another madhhab is only valid under genuine severe necessity, not for personal convenience, as established by ijma.

Talfiq and the Condition of Not Violating Ijma
Rasm al-mufti rule on talfiq condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prohibited and permissible talfiq based on whether it violates scholarly consensus (ijma) and apply this condition to concrete cases.

Core Idea
Talfiq that produces a ruling accepted by no imam is prohibited by consensus.

When a mufti or layperson follows multiple madhhabs to resolve a difficulty, they must ensure their composite ruling does not violate ijma. This lesson unpacks the condition articulated by the majority: talfiq that leads to a ruling no imam validates is invalid. Through clear examples from wudu and prayer, you will learn to differentiate permissible talfiq (in separate actions) from prohibited talfiq (in a single action that contravenes consensus).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
The passage states that talfiq must not violate ijma. Understand that this is a condition accepted by the majority of scholars and stressed by Sahib al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
Ijma here refers to the consensus of the mujtahid imams on the invalidity of a certain combination.
2
Classify the action as single or separate
Determine whether the talfiq occurs within one act of worship (e.g., a single wudu) or across two independent acts (e.g., wudu and prayer). The ruling depends on this classification.
In a single act, combining contradictory elements from different madhhabs produces a hybrid that no imam accepts.
3
Check for violation of ijma
In a single act, if the combination results in a ruling that no recognized imam considers valid, it violates ijma and is prohibited. In separate acts, even if each act follows a different madhhab, there is no violation because each act is independently valid according to some imam.
The example given: wudu without sequence (Shafi'i invalid) + wiping less than ¼ head (Hanafi invalid) → no imam validates this wudu → violates ijma.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a case, first identify the acts involved. If the problematic elements are within one act, talfiq is prohibited. If they are in separate acts, talfiq is permissible (though caution may dictate avoidance).
The author avoids even permissible talfiq out of caution, but the legal ruling allows it.

Source Text

اور قیود میں طویل کلام اور کافی اختلاف ہے جن کو ایک مستقل تصنيف التحفيق في التلفیق میں بیان کر کے اعلاء السنن کی کتاب البیوع کے مقدمہ کا جز بنادیا گیا ہے اور ہمارے نزدیک ان اقوال مختلفہ میں سے یہ قول سب سے زیادہ بہتر ہے کہ عمل واحد میں تلفیق خارق للاجماع کی اجازت نہ ہو اور دو عمل علیحدہ ہوں تو اُن میں تلفیق کی اجازت دی جائے اگر چہ ظاہری طور پر اجماع کے خلاف لازم نہ آتا ہو مثلاً کوئی شخص ترتیب کے بغیر وضو کرے تو حضرات شافعیہ کے نزدیک وضو صح نہیں ۔ اور کوئی شخص چو تھائی سر سے کم مسح کرے تو اخاف کے نزدیک وضو نہیں ہوتا پس گر کوئی شخص اس طریقہ سے وضو کرے کہ ترتیب کی رعایت نہ ہو اور چوتھائی سر سے کم کا مسح کرنے تو کسی امام کے نزدیک بھی وضو نہیں ہوا ۔ اور یہ
تلفیق خارق للاجماع ہے ۔ اور اگر کسی نے وضو میں جو تھائی سر سے کم کا مسح کیا اور زمانہ میں فاتحہ خلف الامام نہ پڑھی تو ظاہری طور پر اس صورت میں بھی اجماع کے خلاف لازم آتا ہے کہ (وہ) وضوحضرات شوافع کے مذہب پر ہے، اور نماز احناف کے مذہب پر ہے لیکن وضو ایک علیحدہ عمل ہے اور نما نہ علیحدہ عمل اس وجہ سے یہ تلفیق ممنوع نہیں۔ لیکن احتیاط پیش نظر رکھ کر اس تصنیف میں تلفیق کی دوسری قسم سے بھی بچاؤ رکھا ہے ۔
That and today I adhere to that, it is not from that because that opens the door to tampering with religion and opens the means for making lawful and unlawful according to desires. (Fatwa Ibn Taymiyya, vol. 2, p. 23). And in the chapter of accepting testimony from Radd al-Muhtar: it was said: whoever transfers to the Shafi'i school to marry off his [daughter], I fear he may be stripped of faith due to dishonoring religion with a filthy carcass, to the end of this chapter from al-Minh. And if he transfers to a madhhab as happens and his nature inclines to it for a purpose he obtains, then his testimony is not accepted (Shami). It is clearer from what is in Ta'zirud? al-Matan, so refer to it. And it is explicitly stated by Allama Muhammad bin Ali al-Baydawi in the thirty-eighth narration from the Mukhtasar Maliki fatawa at the end of the treatise. Due to extreme necessity, in this work, permission to act upon the Maliki school is given only in those cases where a definite severe necessity is found, and not where the necessity is not definite severe, so the facilities given in the Maliki school are not utilized. Regarding acting upon another imam's madhhab, there is also this condition according to the majority of scholars: that the talfiq must not violate consensus. To the extent that Sahib al-Durr al-Mukhtar narrated consensus on this with these words: 'Indeed, the patched ruling is invalid by consensus.' And there is lengthy discussion and considerable difference regarding the details and restrictions of this condition, which have been explained in an independent work 'al-Tahqiq fi al-Talfiq' and made part of the introduction to the book of Buyu' in I'la al-Sunan. And according to us, the best of these differing opinions is that talfiq violating consensus is not allowed in a single action, but if there are two separate actions, talfiq between them is allowed even if outwardly it seems contrary to consensus. For example, if someone performs wudu without sequence, according to the Shafi'is the wudu is not valid. And if someone wipes less than a quarter of the head, according to the Hanafis the wudu is not valid. So if a person performs wudu in this way, without observing sequence and wiping less than a quarter of the head, then according to no imam is the wudu valid. This is talfiq violating consensus. And if someone wipes less than a quarter of the head in wudu and in prayer does not recite Fatiha behind the imam, then outwardly this case also seems contrary to consensus because the wudu is according to the Shafi'i school and the prayer is according to the Hanafi school, but since wudu is a separate action and prayer is a separate action, this talfiq is not prohibited. However, keeping caution in view, in this work even the second type of talfiq has been avoided.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule regarding the conditions for following another madhhab and the permissibility of talfiq (patchworking). The author begins by citing Ibn Taymiyya's warning against changing rulings based on desires. He then quotes from Radd al-Muhtar and Shami about the rejection of testimony from someone who switches madhhabs for worldly purposes, indicating the gravity of disrespecting the religion. The core condition presented is that talfiq must not violate scholarly consensus (ijma). The author explains two scenarios: (1) In a single action, combining rulings from different imams such that no imam would validate the act is prohibited talfiq. Example: performing wudu without sequence (invalid in Shafi'i) and wiping less than a quarter of the head (invalid in Hanafi) – no imam accepts this wudu. (2) In separate actions, talfiq is allowed even if it outwardly contradicts consensus, because each action is considered independently. Example: wiping less than a quarter of the head (Hanafi view) and not reciting Fatiha behind the imam (Hanafi view) are separate acts. However, due to caution, the author states that he has avoided even this type of talfiq in the present work.

Key Terms
تلفيق (talfiq): Combining rulings from different madhhabs in legal practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of prohibited talfiq that violates ijma?
A) Wiping less than a quarter of the head (Hanafi) and not reciting Fatiha behind the imam (Hanafi) in separate acts.
B) Wiping less than a quarter of the head (Hanafi) and omitting sequence in wudu (Shafi'i) in a single wudu.
C) Performing wudu with sequence (Shafi'i) and reciting Fatiha behind the imam (Shafi'i).
D) Following the Maliki school in a case of extreme necessity.

Key Takeaway
Talfiq is only prohibited when it produces a ruling that no imam accepts in a single action; talfiq across independent acts is permissible.

Ruling on Talfiq in Single vs. Separate Acts
Explains two types of talfiq and which are permissible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of talfiq, explain why single-act talfiq that breaks consensus is prohibited, and apply the rule to distinguish permissible from impermissible talfiq.

Core Idea
Talfiq that yields a ruling no school accepts is invalid; talfiq across independent acts is allowed.

This passage presents a key rasm al-mufti rule: when can a mufti combine rulings from different madhhabs? The answer hinges on whether the combination occurs within a single act or spans separate acts. Understanding this distinction prevents fatwas that inadvertently violate consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of talfiq
The author explicitly distinguishes between talfiq in a single action (عمل واحد) and talfiq in two separate actions (عملین علیحدہ).
2
Understand the condition for prohibition
Single-act talfiq is prohibited only when it results in a ruling that breaks consensus (خارق للاجماع) — i.e., no recognized madhhab validates the combined act.
The key term is 'khāriq li'l-ijmā'.
3
Examine the invalid wudu example
Combining absence of order (Shafi'i condition) with insufficient head wipe (Hanafi condition) yields a wudu that no imam deems valid. This is impermissible talfiq.
Both conditions are necessary in the respective schools; the combination fails all.
4
Contrast with separate acts example
Performing wudu according to one school (Shafi'i) and prayer according to another (Hanafi) involves two independent acts. Each act is valid in its own school, so the talfiq is permitted even if the overall combination appears to go against consensus.
The author notes that as a precaution, he avoids even this type in his work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور قیود میں طویل کلام اور کافی اختلاف ہے جن کو ایک مستقل تصنيف التحفيق في التلفیق میں بیان کر کے اعلاء السنن کی کتاب البیوع کے مقدمہ کا جز بنادیا گیا ہے اور ہمارے نزدیک ان اقوال مختلفہ میں سے یہ قول سب سے زیادہ بہتر ہے کہ عمل واحد میں تلفیق خارق للاجماع کی اجازت نہ ہو اور دو عمل علیحدہ ہوں تو اُن میں تلفیق کی اجازت دی جائے اگر چہ ظاہری طور پر اجماع کے خلاف لازم نہ آتا ہو مثلاً کوئی شخص ترتیب کے بغیر وضو کرے تو حضرات شافعیہ کے نزدیک وضو صح نہیں ۔ اور کوئی شخص چوتھائی سر سے کم مسح کرے تو احناف کے نزدیک وضو نہیں ہوتا پس گر کوئی شخص اس طریقہ سے وضو کرے کہ ترتیب کی رعایت نہ ہو اور چوتھائی سر سے کم کا مسح کرنے تو کسی امام کے نزدیک بھی وضو نہیں ہوا ۔ اور یہ تلفیق خارق للاجماع ہے ۔ اور اگر کسی نے وضو میں چوتھائی سر سے کم کا مسح کیا اور نماز میں فاتحہ خلف الامام نہ پڑھی تو ظاہری طور پر اس صورت میں بھی اجماع کے خلاف لازم آتا ہے کہ (وہ) وضو حضرات شوافع کے مذہب پر ہے، اور نماز احناف کے مذہب پر ہے لیکن وضو ایک علیحدہ عمل ہے اور نماز علیحدہ عمل اس وجہ سے یہ تلفیق ممنوع نہیں۔ لیکن احتیاط پیش نظر رکھ کر اس تصنیف میں تلفیق کی دوسری قسم سے بھی بچاؤ رکھا ہے ۔
And there is lengthy discussion and significant disagreement regarding restrictions. These have been explained in an independent work, al-Tahqeeq fi al-Talfiq, and made part of the introduction to the Book of Sales in I'la al-Sunan. According to us, among these various opinions, the best saying is that talfiq in a single action that breaks consensus is not permitted, and if there are two separate actions, then talfiq between them is permitted, even if outwardly it necessitates going against consensus. For example, if a person performs wudu without observing order, according to the Shafi'is the wudu is not valid; and if a person wipes less than a quarter of the head, according to the Hanafis the wudu is not valid. So if a person performs wudu in a manner that neither observes order nor wipes a quarter of the head, then according to no imam did the wudu occur, and this is talfiq that breaks consensus. And if someone wiped less than a quarter of the head in wudu and did not recite Fatiha behind the imam in prayer, then outwardly this case also necessitates going against consensus, because the wudu is according to the Shafi'i school and the prayer is according to the Hanafi school, but wudu is a separate action and prayer is a separate action; for this reason this talfiq is not prohibited. However, as a precaution, in this work we have also avoided the second type of talfiq.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule regarding talfiq (piecing together rulings from different madhhabs). The author classifies talfiq into two types: talfiq in a single act and talfiq across two separate acts. The relied-upon position is that talfiq in a single act that breaks consensus (i.e., results in a ruling that no scholar accepts) is impermissible. The example given is wudu that combines the Shafi'i condition of order and the Hanafi condition of wiping a quarter of the head—neither school validates it, so it becomes invalid by consensus. However, talfiq in two separate acts—such as performing wudu according to one school and prayer according to another—is permitted, even if the combination appears to violate consensus in a general sense, because each act stands alone. Nevertheless, as a matter of caution, the author avoids even this type of talfiq in the present work. The authority weight is from later fatwa works (Fatawa/Mutakhkhirin).

Key Terms
تلفیق (talfīq): Piecing together or combining rulings from different madhhabs in a single case.
خارق للاجماع (khāriq li'l-ijmā'): Something that violates or breaks the consensus of the scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is impermissible because it breaks consensus.
B) It is permissible because the acts are independent.
C) It is permissible only if the mufti follows one school for life.
D) It is impermissible in all cases.

Key Takeaway
Talfiq is permitted in separate independent actions, but not in a single action if it results in a ruling that no madhhab accepts.

Methodology for Choosing Conditional Divorce Wording
Author explains fiqh basis for specific tafwid wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that laypeople must use the mufti's prescribed phrases for conditional divorce and explain the rationale for limiting wording to a single divorce.

Core Idea
A layperson may not invent his own conditional divorce wording; he must use the mufti's approved phrases.

In this passage, Maulana Thanwi explains the careful reasoning behind the specific Arabic phrases he recommends for delegation of divorce (tafwid al-talaq) in kabinnama documents. He aims to give the wife power to divorce only once, not three times, and to avoid immediate effect tied to the condition's council. Since Urdu idioms vary, he provides underlying Arabic fiqh details for scholars to analogize, but strictly forbids laypeople from deviating from his proposals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's goal
The author wants the wife to have power to give only one divorce, not three, and not tied to the council of the condition's existence. This is the intended legal effect (thamara).
2
Analyze the author's wording choice
The author chooses phrases that, according to common usage (muhawarat) of both parties, achieve that goal. Note that the condition is separate from the council (majlis) to avoid immediate effect.
3
Recognize the limitation for laypeople
The author states he cannot list all possible conditional words due to varying Urdu idioms. Therefore, he provides the Arabic fiqh detail for scholars to work from. Laypeople must stick to his proposed phrases and consult scholars if they need other words.
4
Derive the mufti's methodological rule
A mufti, when designing a conditional divorce clause, must consider the following: (1) limit the number of divorces granted, (2) avoid tying the delegation to the council if immediate effect is undesirable, (3) use wording that aligns with the common idiom of the parties, and (4) provide a clear path for scholars to analogize from established Arabic rulings.
This is a practical application of rasm al-mufti (rules for mufti).
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
If a layperson comes to you with a self-written conditional divorce phrase, you must determine whether it falls within the approved set. If not, you must either reject it or, if you are a scholar, analogize from the Arabic detail provided. The layperson cannot decide on their own.

جائے اور نہ اس قدر وسعت ہو کہ عورت کو تینوں طلاقیں واقع کرنے کا اختیار مل جائے اسی وجہ سے ہم نے کا بین ناموں میں اور گذشتہ مثالوں میں اس قسم کے الفاظ استعمال کیئے ہیں جو کہ باتفاق محاورات فریقین کی رعایت اور مصلحت پر مشتمل ہیں ۔ ان الفاظ سے صرف ایک ہی مرتبہ طلاق دینے کا اختیار حاصل ہوگا ، لیکن وجود شرط کی مجلس کے ساتھ نہ ہوگا، اور اردو کے محاورات مختلف ہونے کی وجہ سے تمام الفاظ مشروط کا حکم بیان نہ ہو سکا۔ اس وجہ سے عربی الفاظ کی تفصیل نقل کی جاتی ہے تا کہ اہل علم ضرورت کے وقت اس تفصیل میں اور متکلم کے محاورہ میں غور سے مطابقت کر کے بقیہ شرط کے الفاظ کا حکم متعین کرسکیں اور عام لوگوں کے لیے لازم ہے کہ ہم نے جو الفاظ تجویز کئے ہیں ان کی رعایت پیش نظر رکھیں اور اگر ان کے علاوہ کسی لفظ کا حکم معلوم ہونے کی ضرورت پیش آجائے تو علماء کرام سے دریافت کرلیں صرف اپنی نه بان دانی بر اعتماد کر کے خود فیصلہ کرنا جائز نہیں۔
It should not be such that the woman gets the power to effect three divorces. For this reason, in the marriage contracts (kabinnamas) and in previous examples, we have used such words which, according to the common usage of both parties, are based on consideration and expediency. With these words, she will only have the power to give one divorce, but it will not be tied to the council of the condition's existence. Since Urdu idioms are different, the ruling of all conditional words could not be stated. Therefore, the detailed rulings of Arabic words are quoted so that scholars, when necessary, can determine the ruling of remaining conditional words by careful matching of this detail with the speaker's idiom. And for the common people, it is necessary to keep in view the words we have proposed. If they need to know the ruling of another word, they should ask the scholars; it is not permissible to decide on their own relying only on their ignorance.

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note (rasm al-mufti) from the author explaining why he chose particular Arabic/Urdu phrases in the kabinnama examples. His goal is to give the wife power to divorce only once, not three times, and to avoid the condition being tied to the council (majlis) with immediate effect. Since Urdu idioms vary, he cannot list every phrase; instead, he provides the underlying Arabic fiqh details for scholars to analogize. For laypeople, he insists on using only the phrases he has proposed and warns against self-ijtihad—a critical rule for muftis and common people alike.

Key Terms
تفویض طلاق (tafwid al-talaq): Delegation of the power of divorce from husband to wife, subject to specified conditions.
محاورات (muhawarat): Idiomatic expressions and customary speech patterns of a language or community.

Qadi's Decree Required for Separation in Non-Muslim Lands
Rule requiring qadi's decree for separations, with two automatic exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a qadi's decree is necessary for legal separation (tafriq) in non-Muslim lands and identify the two automatic exceptions (apostasy and conversion to Islam).

Core Idea
Qadi's decree is mandatory for most separations; only apostasy and conversion dissolve marriage automatically.

This passage from Hilatun Najizah addresses a critical practical question for Hanafi muftis in non-Muslim jurisdictions: when does a woman need a court order to end a marriage? The author establishes the general rule that a qadi's judgment (qada) is essential, then clarifies a potential confusion arising from a reported text that a fatwa suffices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Read the passage carefully: what does the author state as a condition for legal separation (tafriq shar'i) in non-Muslim lands? The answer is explicit: 'قضاء قاضی شرط ہے' (qadi's decree is a condition).
The rule applies to grounds like insanity, disappearance, neglect of rights, etc.
2
Recognize the exceptions
The text mentions two cases that do not require a qadi's decree: apostasy (irtidad) of the husband and conversion to Islam of one spouse (islam ahad al-zawjayn). Note that these cases cause automatic separation.
These are ba'in (irrevocable) separations.
3
Understand the doubt raised
Some scholars ('اخواننا') argued based on a text from 'قضاء تتقم الحامدية' that the fatwa of a faqih suffices. Identify the statement they cited: 'لان الفتوى الفقية' (because the fatwa of a knowledgeable person is enough).
This suggests that a mufti's opinion could replace a qadi's verdict.
4
Analyze the author's refutation
The author responds by limiting that statement to the two exceptions (apostasy and conversion). For all other grounds, the qadi's decree remains necessary. This refines the understanding of the earlier text.
The author uses the term 'فائدة دافعه لشبهة' – a benefit that repels a doubt.
5
Apply the rule in practice
Imagine a woman whose husband is insane. According to the rule, she must seek a qadi's judgment. A mufti cannot simply issue a fatwa of separation. Contrast with a case of apostasy: no court order is needed.
This distinction is crucial for muftis giving guidance in non-Muslim countries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ہندستان اور دیگر غیراسلامی ممالک میں قاضی کے فیصلہ کی شرعی حیثیت گذشتہ صفحات میں مذکورہ تفریق شرعی کے اسباب کی بناء پر قضاء قاضی شرط ہے (مطلب یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی عورت کا شوہر مجنون ہو یا لا پتہ ہو گیا ہو یا حقوق سے لا پرواہ ہو یا غائب ہو گیا ہو تو ایسی عورت قاضی کے ہاں طلاق حاصل کرنے کے لیے دعوی کرے ، اور عورت اس کے ولی فسخ نکاح میں خود مختار نہیں بلکہ شرط یہ ہے کہ قاضی کی عدالت میں مقدمہ دائر کریں اور قاضی با ضابطہ تحقیق شرعی شہادت وغیرہ کے ذریعہ کرنے کے بعد فیصلہ کرے اس کاروائی کے بغیر مذکورہ مسائل میں سے کسی مسئلہ میں تفریق ریعنی طلاق واقع نہیں ہو سکتی۔
فائدة دافعه لشبهة في اشتراط القضاء.
اعلوانه قد استدال بعض أهل العلم من اخواننا بما ورد فى قضاء تتقم الحامدية ( صلح ) من قوله لان الفتوى الفقية لے ان کے علاوہ بعض صورت میں ایسی بھی ہیں جو فضا ئے قاضی کی محتاج نہیں۔ بلکہ ان میں خود بخود نکاح سے علیحدگی ہو جاتی ہے اُن میں سے دو صورتیں تو رسالہ ہا کے منیم حکم الازدواج میں مفصل لکھی گئی ہیں۔ ایک ارتداد شوہر دوسرے اسلام احد الزوجین لہذا منیجمہ موصوفہ کو ضرور ملاحظہ کیا جادے اور فرقت کی سب صورتیں ایک نظم میں صاحب بہر نے جمع کر دی ہیں وہ نظم اس رسالہ حیلہ ناجزہ کے تتہ میں درج ہے وہ منہ نے
In India and other non-Islamic countries, the legal status of the qadi's decision: based on the causes of legal separation mentioned in previous pages, the decree of a qadi is a condition. (Meaning that if a woman's husband is insane, or has gone missing, or is neglectful of rights, or has disappeared, then such a woman should file a claim for divorce before a qadi. The woman is not independent in annulling the marriage; rather, the condition is that she must file a case in the qadi's court, and after proper investigation, testimony, etc., the qadi will decide. Without this procedure, separation (i.e., divorce) cannot occur in any of these issues.)
Benefit that repels a doubt concerning the condition of qadi's decree.
Because some scholars from our brethren have argued based on what is reported in the section on qadi's jurisdiction in Al-Hamidiyyah (?) from his statement: 'because the fatwa of a faqih suffices'. However, besides those, there are some cases that do not require a qadi's decree; rather, in them separation from the marriage occurs automatically. Two of those cases have been detailed in the treatise 'Minhaj al-Hukm al-Izdawaj' (or similar). One is apostasy of the husband, the second is conversion to Islam of one spouse. So the aforementioned treatise must be consulted. And all forms of separation have been collected in a poem by the author of 'Al-Bahr' (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq); that poem is included in the appendix of this treatise 'Hilatun Najizah'.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage addresses the rule of qadi's decree (qada) in non-Muslim countries. The author establishes that for legal separation due to specific grounds (insanity, disappearance, neglect of rights, etc.), a qadi's judgment is mandatory. Without it, no divorce is valid. This reflects the Hanafi position that a woman cannot dissolve her marriage unilaterally in such cases; she must approach a competent court. Then the author provides a benefit (fa'idah) that repels a doubt: some scholars have argued based on a text from a Hanafi work (perhaps Al-Hidayah or similar) that the fatwa of a faqih (mufti) is sufficient. The author refutes this by clarifying that there are only two exceptions where separation occurs automatically without qadi's decree: (1) apostasy (irtidad) of the husband, and (2) conversion to Islam of one spouse (when the other remains non-Muslim). In all other cases, qadi's decree is necessary. The author refers to a detailed treatise and a poem summarizing all forms of separation. This is a methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) concerning the limits of judicial intervention vs. automatic legal effects.

Key Terms
قضاء قاضی (qada al-qadi): The formal judgment of a Muslim judge, required for legal separation.
تفریق شرعی (tafriq shar'i): Legal separation of spouses by court order.
ارتداد (irtidad): Apostasy from Islam; causes automatic separation without qadi's decree.
اسلام احد الزوجین (islam ahad al-zawjayn): Conversion to Islam by one spouse while the other remains non-Muslim; leads to automatic separation if the other does not convert.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Apostasy and conversion to Islam
C) Neglect of rights and disappearance

Key Takeaway
For legal separations in non-Muslim lands, a qadi's decree is mandatory except when apostasy or conversion occurs; fatwa alone is insufficient.

Fatwa vs Qada: The Mufti's Opinion Does Not Replace the Judge's Verdict
Refuting the claim that fatwa equals qada.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fatwa and qada and apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule that a fatwa does not substitute a qadi's judgment.

Core Idea
A mufti's fatwa is advisory opinion, not judicial verdict — qada alone carries binding enforcement.

In this passage, the author refutes a claim that a mufti's fatwa stands in place of a qadi's judgment. He argues that fatwa and qada operate at different levels: fatwa is personal guidance for the layperson, while qada is a binding institutional decision. Understanding this distinction is foundational for a mufti to know the limits of his authority and to avoid overstepping into judicial territory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Find the opposing argument: that a mufti's fatwa suffices instead of a qadi's judgment in all cases, based on a transmitted statement.
The claim is attributed to 'some of our brethren' citing a text (possibly Qada Taqiyya al-Hamidiyya).
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The author asserts that fatwa is merely opinion (ra'y) and ijtihad; qada has pillars including a judge and cannot be replaced. The proof is the logical consequence that if fatwa were equivalent, the entire system of qada would collapse.
The Hanafi position is based on the fundamental distinction between qada (judicial) and diyana (religious duty).
3
Analyze the opponent's proof
The opponent relies on the apparent literal meaning of a statement that appears to equate fatwa with qada. The author argues that this statement is misinterpreted.
The statement is qualified by 'for the ignorant person' (li'l-jahil), indicating it is not universal.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The author deconstructs the opponent's claim: (1) universalizing it would nullify the purpose of qada; (2) the context (siyaq and sabaq) shows it refers to specific cases; (3) the qualification 'for the ignorant' proves it is not general.
The correct interpretation is that the fatwa stands like a qadi's judgment only for the layperson in specific matters (e.g., option of annulment due to defects).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarat al-khilaf)
In practice, a woman seeking divorce due to defects must go to a qadi; a mufti's fatwa alone does not effect legal separation. The mufti must advise her to seek judicial recourse.
This ruling is especially relevant in non-Muslim countries where the Islamic judiciary may not be available.

اعلوانه قد استدال بعض أهل العلم من اخواننا بما ورد فى قضاء تتقم الحامدية ( صلح ) من قوله لان الفتوى الفقية لے ان کے علاوہ بعض صورت میں ایسی بھی ہیں جو فضا ئے قاضی کی محتاج نہیں۔ بلکہ ان میں خود بخود نکاح سے علیحدگی ہو جاتی ہے اُن میں سے دو صورتیں تو رسالہ ہا کے منیم حکم الازدواج میں مفصل لکھی گئی ہیں۔ ایک ارتداد شوہر دوسرے اسلام احد الزوجین لہذا منیجمہ موصوفہ کو ضرور ملاحظہ کیا جادے اور فرقت کی سب صورتیں ایک نظم میں صاحب بہر نے جمع کر دی ہیں وہ نظم اس رسالہ حیلہ ناجزہ کے تتہ میں درج ہے وہ منہ نے

The legal status of a qadi's judgment in India and other non-Muslim countries: Based on the causes of separation mentioned in previous pages, the judgment of a qadi is a condition. Meaning: if a woman's husband is insane, missing, neglectful of rights, or absent, she must file a lawsuit before the qadi to obtain divorce. The woman or her guardian is not independent in annulment; rather, it is required that they file the case in the qadi's court, and the qadi, after conducting a proper shari'a investigation including testimony, issues a judgment. Without this procedure, separation/divorce does not occur in any of those issues. Beneficial point to repel doubt about the condition of qada. Know that some scholars among our brethren have argued with what is narrated in the qada of Taqiyya al-Hamidiyya (?) from his statement: 'for the fatwa of the jurist...' Besides those, there are also some cases that do not need the judgment of a qadi; rather, separation from marriage occurs automatically. Two of those types are detailed in the treatise 'Muhimmat Hukm al-Izdawaj': one, apostasy of the husband; second, conversion to Islam of one of the spouses. Therefore, that compilation must be seen. And all forms of separation have been collected in a poem by Sahib Bahar; that poem is recorded at the end of this treatise 'Hila Najaiza'. He then said: For the ignorant person, it is like the ruling of the appointed qadi or the ruling of the arbitrator. However, regarding those issues in which qada is required, such as the option of annulment due to defects and lack of equality and others, the fatwa of the mufti suffices and stands in place of qada. For from its apparent meaning is understood that the fatwa of a jurist stands in place of the judgment of the qadi in all cases. But you know that if it were so, the doors of qada would be entirely closed, and there would remain no meaning for the condition of qada in many rulings, and there would be no difference between the rulings of qada and religious duty (diyana), for the ruling of diyana is the fatwa, as is not hidden from anyone who has the slightest grasp of the discipline. Therefore it becomes clear that applying this statement universally closes this section of the fiqh doors. And then one should not think, who has even the slightest connection to fiqh, to take this statement as a barrier to resort to in order to escape the narrowness of qada. The truth, which is real and from which deviation is not permissible, is that the rank of fatwa is the rank of opinion and ijtihad. So it is necessary for the ignorant person to act upon the fatwa in what is required for the person of opinion and ijtihad to act upon his opinion and ijtihad. And the fatwa is not of the same status as qada, because qada has pillars, including the judge. The jurists have stated explicitly that it is not permissible for the ignorant person to act upon the fatwa in what is not permissible for the person of opinion to act upon his opinion without the end of a judicial session. And then it is necessary to interpret the mentioned transmitted statement to remove difficulty and to direct it in a way that does not contradict the rules. If the interpretation is not accepted, it must be rejected. So we say in directing the expression (and upon Allah is reliance and in Him is trust): the mistake arose from not looking at the context (siyaq and sabaq). Whoever refers back to the original book and deeply examines its context and style will not doubt that it is a clarification of a specific issue, not a general one. And for this reason, we see that the scholars have used these words in other chapters and general cases? And among the evidence is that they limited it to the ignorant person; if the fatwa were of the status of qada in all rulings, this qualification would have no meaning, for qada is binding even on the ignorant.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement on the distinction between fatwa and qada. It begins by stating the general rule in non-Muslim lands: for certain types of marital separation (e.g., husband insane, missing, or irresponsible), the intervention of a qadi is mandatory. The woman cannot unilaterally annul the marriage; she must file a case, and the qadi after proper investigation issues a judgment. Without this, no legal separation occurs. Then the author addresses a doubt raised by some scholars who rely on a statement from 'Qada Taqiyya al-Hamidiyya' that a mufti's fatwa suffices in place of a qadi's judgment in all cases. The author refutes this absolutely. He argues that if such equivalence existed, the entire institution of qada would be rendered meaningless, and there would be no difference between the binding judgment of a court and the mere advisory opinion of a mufti. He emphasizes that fatwa is at the level of ra'y (opinion) and ijtihad, and only binds the person who asks (the jahil) in matters of personal duty, but it does not carry the enforcement power of a qadi's judgment. He explains that qada has pillars (arkan), including the judge (hakim), and cannot be replaced by a mufti. The correct interpretation of the transmitted saying is that it refers only to specific cases (like option of annulment due to defects or lack of kafa'a), not universally. The author then provides a hermeneutical principle: the statement must be interpreted in light of its context (siyaq and sabaq), otherwise it leads to contradiction with established fiqh rules. He notes that the qualification 'for the ignorant' (jahil) itself indicates that the fatwa is not general, because qada binds even the ignorant. So the passage establishes a clear rasm rule: fatwa is not equivalent to qada; it is a tool for guidance, not a substitute for judicial authority.

Key Terms
فتوى (fatwa): A non-binding legal opinion issued by a mufti based on ijtihad, which the layperson must follow in matters of personal obligation but which has no judicial enforcement power.
قضاء (qada): The adjudication by a qadi that is binding and enforceable, with specific pillars (arkan) including the judge and formal proceedings.
جاهل (jahil): A person who is not a mujtahid and thus must follow the fatwa of a qualified jurist in matters of ijtihad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a mufti's fatwa not equivalent to a qadi's judgment?
A) Because a qadi is always more knowledgeable than a mufti
B) Because fatwa is merely opinion and lacks the binding enforcement pillars of qada
C) Because fatwa is only valid for women
D) Because the Hanafi school rejects all fatwas in legal disputes

Key Takeaway
A fatwa is a personal legal opinion that binds only the layperson in matters of religious duty, while a qadi's judgment is a binding and enforceable judicial verdict.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Mufti's Fatwa as Equivalent to a Judge's Verdict for the Layperson
Explaining the maxim and its application in taqlid of another madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'The fatwa of the jurist for the ignorant is in the position of the ruling of the judge' by distinguishing between matters that require a judge's verdict and those that do not, and evaluate when a layperson may act on a mufti's fatwa from another madhhab.

Core Idea
A mufti's fatwa binds a layperson like a judge's ruling, but only in matters where the other madhhab does not require qada.

This lesson unpacks a foundational principle of Hanafi ifta: the authority of the mufti's opinion for the layperson who follows another madhhab. The principle is not absolute; its boundary is drawn by whether the legal matter requires judicial enforcement. We will explore the maxim, its supporting examples, its exception, and its practical application in contexts like modern India.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Begin by reading the core maxim: 'The fatwa of the jurist for the ignorant is in the position of the ruling of the judge.' Identify the key terms: mufti (jurist), jahil (layperson who cannot derive rulings), and qadi (judge).
The maxim is a minor (sughra) legal maxim governing the authority of fatwa in taqlid of another madhhab.
2
Identify the Condition for Application
The maxim applies only when the ruling in the other madhhab is not conditional upon a judge's verdict. If the ruling requires a judge, the fatwa alone is insufficient.
The condition is derived from the phrase 'إذا كان الحكم غير مشروط بالقضاء' in the passage.
3
Analyse the Examples
The passage gives two categories: (a) matters where no qada needed (e.g., annulling an added oath, revocability of divorce in written divorces) – layperson may act on fatwa alone; (b) matters requiring qada (e.g., maintenance disputes, disputes over keys) – layperson needs both fatwa and judge's ruling.
These examples are from the Shafi'i madhhab; the principle applies to any madhhab.
4
Understand the Exception (Istithna)
When a ruling is conditional upon qada, the fatwa of a mufti from another madhhab cannot be acted upon unless all conditions of that madhhab are fulfilled, including obtaining a judge's verdict from that school.
This prevents the layperson from cherry-picking rulings without satisfying procedural requirements.
5
Apply to Modern Context
In territories without a Shari'ah judge, a Muslim magistrate who rules according to Shari'ah can substitute for the qadi. However, a non-Muslim magistrate cannot judge Muslims.
This application is from the Urdu section of the passage, citing Durr al-Mukhtar.

Similarly, it is effective upon the scholar and mujtahid... and among them is that it is not permissible for a layperson to act upon the madhhab of another by the fatwa of a mufti, just as it is permissible for him to act upon it by the verdict of a judge. However, if the mufti gives a fatwa according to another madhhab in an incident, and if the ruling in that madhhab is not conditional upon a judicial verdict, it suffices for the layperson to act upon it merely by the fatwa without needing a judge's verdict, as in our case of annulling an added oath and the permissibility of returning to a wife in divorces via written documents, for according to al-Shafi'i it is not conditional upon a judge. So if a mufti gives a fatwa adopting the Shafi'i madhhab in these matters, it is permissible for the layperson to act upon it merely by the fatwa. Thus, the fatwa in such rulings is like a judge's verdict, since it becomes permissible for the layperson to act upon another madhhab. And this is the meaning of their statement: 'The fatwa of the jurist for the ignorant is in the position of the ruling of the judge who has authority' meaning in such matters which are of the type of ijtihad that do not require a judge. But if the ruling in that madhhab is conditional upon a judge, as in the matter of the keys... and the two who are impudent in maintenance and the like, then if a mufti gives a fatwa in such cases according to another madhhab, it is not permissible for the layperson to act upon it unless he fulfills the conditions of that madhhab, among which is the judge's verdict.
And what we said is supported in 'Intisar al-Mahdawiyya' by our master Sheikh Muhammad al-Abbasi al-Khafi?, the mufti of the Egyptian lands, where he said: 'The fatwa of the jurist for the ignorant is in the position of the judge's ruling' and its meaning is that the fatwa takes the place of what was mentioned in obligating action upon it for the one seeking the fatwa himself, based on their statement in another phrasing: 'The statement of the mufti for the ignorant is in the position of his own opinion and ijtihad.' And he explicitly stated therein that this opinion follows the opinion of the judge if he rules for or against him, contrary to his own opinion. (Then he cited the statement of Shams al-A'imma which we presented from Tanqih al-Hamidiyya until he said): And his statement therein: 'It is within his capacity to retain her' is evidence that this matter is a religious matter... the Khurtanti? Mahdawi? book of divorce, a section...
...his wife by 'haram'. Then (refer to p. 33 and p. 23 vol. 1). This is what occurred to us, and Allah knows best. I say: With this understanding, the answer to what is in the discussion of sighting the crescent from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya' is as follows: 'And a scholar and trustworthy person in a town where there is no judge acts as his substitute (i.e., the judge), because this is also specific to the matters of his statement, meaning in the chapter of sighting the crescent which does not require a judge, not to all cases, as is not hidden from the student. This is the correct view, and the matter is with Allah, and to Him is the return in every chapter.'
And in the current situation in Hindustan (India), since generally Shari'ah judges are not found, before explaining these issues, such situations are described that can be found in India, and those situations are as follows:
A Muslim magistrate's decision.
In areas of India where Shari'ah judges are present, the matter is easy, and in the regions of the government where there are no Shari'ah judges, those officials like district magistrates etc., who are authorized by the government to decide in such matters, if they are Muslims and they decide according to Shari'ah law, then their decision also takes the place of the decision of a Shari'ah judge. As it is in Durr al-Mukhtar: 'It is permissible to accept the appointment of qada from a just or oppressive ruler, even if he is a disbeliever, male or female, etc.' But if in any place the deciding ruler is unreliable, his ruling does not effect (change) anything (shar'an, etc.). 'Because a disbeliever is not qualified to judge over a Muslim, as is explicitly stated in all books of fiqh.' (i.e., a non-Muslim is not authorized to decide for Muslims, as is clarified in books of fiqh.)
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage addresses a key rasm al-mufti principle: the layperson's obligation to follow a mufti's fatwa when acting upon a different madhhab. The author explains that a mufti's fatwa is equivalent to a judge's verdict for the layperson in matters that do not require judicial proceedings (e.g., fasting, divorce by written documents). The example given is the Shafi'i ruling on annulling an added oath and revocability of divorce—matters that are purely religious and do not need a judge's intervention. However, if the matter requires a judge (e.g., disputes over maintenance), the layperson cannot act solely on a mufti's fatwa from another madhhab; he must also obtain a judge's ruling according to that madhhab's conditions. The author then cites an Egyptian mufti to reinforce that the fatwa is like a judge's ruling in obligating action on the seeker. Finally, the commentary applies this to India's context: a Muslim magistrate who rules according to Shari'ah can substitute for a Shari'ah judge, but a non-Muslim cannot judge Muslims. The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah as reflected in Durr al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
مفتي (mufti): The jurist who issues fatwas; in this context, one whose fatwa is authoritative for the layperson.
جاهل (jahil): A layperson who does not have the ability to derive rulings; the one seeking the fatwa.
قضاء (qada): Judicial ruling by a qualified judge; in this passage, it is distinguished from fatwa in that some rulings require a judge's enforcement.
فتوى (fatwa): A non-binding but authoritative legal opinion; in this context, it is binding upon the layperson in matters that do not require qada.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Validity of a Muslim Magistrate's Judgment as Substitute for a Qadi
Ruling on Muslim magistrate as Qadi substitute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a Muslim magistrate's judgment is equivalent to a Qadi's when he rules by Sharia in the absence of a formal Qadi.

Core Idea
A Muslim magistrate's Sharia-based judgment substitutes for a Qadi's ruling when no Qadi is available.

In many regions, a formally appointed Shari'a Qadi may not be available. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that permits a Muslim magistrate, authorized by the government, to issue judgments that are legally equivalent to those of a Qadi, provided he rules according to Islamic law. The passage draws from classical Hanafi sources and applies them to contemporary contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Recognize the situation: a Muslim magistrate is acting as a judge in the absence of a formal Shari'a Qadi. The question is whether his judgment is valid.
2
State the condition for validity
The condition is that the magistrate must be Muslim and must rule according to Sharia law. If he rules by man-made law, his judgment does not carry the same weight.
This condition is derived from the principle that judgment must be based on divine law.
3
Trace the dalil
The author cites Durr Mukhtar, which states that judgment can emanate from any ruler, just or unjust, even a non-Muslim. This establishes the general permissibility of judgments from non-Qadi authorities, but the specific condition for equivalence is that the judge rules by Sharia.
The dalil is a general principle, not a specific hadith.
4
Apply to the fatwa hierarchy
Determine the mufta bihi: the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that a Muslim magistrate's ruling is valid and binding if he adheres to Sharia. This is based on necessity and the broader principle of valid judgment.
This is a practical solution (hila) for Muslims living under non-Islamic governance.

(his wife with haram?) (reference) This is what seemed correct to us, and Allah knows best. I say: with this understanding, the answer to what is in the discussion of moon sighting from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya, its text: 'And a trustworthy scholar in a town where there is no governor is in his position (meaning the judge).' So it is also specific to matters like the chapter of moon sighting where there is no condition of judgment, not general cases. This is the correct view and the matter is in Allah's hands. In India, in areas where Shari'a Qadis are found, the matter is easy, and in areas under government where Shari'a Qadis are not present, those officials like the magistrate who are authorized by the government to judge such matters, if they are Muslim and they judge according to Sharia law, their judgment also becomes equivalent to the judgment of a Shari'a Qadi. As it is in Durr Mukhtar: 'It is permissible for judgment to emanate from a just or unjust ruler, even if he is a disbeliever, a man, a Muslim, or otherwise.'

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) regarding the validity of a Muslim magistrate's judgment in the absence of a proper Shari'a Qadi. The author begins by referencing an earlier discussion on moon sighting, establishing the principle that a trustworthy scholar in a town without a governor fulfills the role of a judge in specific matters. He then applies this principle to the contemporary context of India, where Shari'a Qadis are often unavailable. The key ruling: if a Muslim magistrate, authorized by the government, rules according to Sharia law, his judgment is considered equivalent to that of a Shari'a Qadi. The author supports this by citing Durr Mukhtar, which states that judgment can be issued by any ruler, just or unjust, even a non-Muslim. However, the emphasis here is on the Muslim magistrate's adherence to Sharia. This is a practical solution (hila) for Muslims living under non-Sharia governance.

قاضی شرع (Qadi Shar'): A judge appointed according to Islamic law to adjudicate disputes.
مجسٹریٹ (Magistrate): A government-appointed official authorized to judge cases, not necessarily a Shari'a Qadi.

Non-Muslim Judge Cannot Rule on a Muslim
Ruling: non-Muslim judge's verdict void for Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a non-Muslim judge's decision regarding a Muslim is invalid and identify the conditions for a valid judgment.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim cannot judge a Muslim; any such ruling is void regardless of Muslim consent.

Islamic judiciary requires competence and impartiality. The Hanafi school holds that a non-Muslim lacks the authority (wilāyah) to adjudicate matters involving Muslims. This lesson examines the textual basis and practical implications of this rule, including a case of impotence and khul'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal rule
Extract the main ruling: a disbeliever (kāfir) is not qualified to judge a Muslim. This is stated explicitly in all books of fiqh.
The passage uses 'غير معتبر' (not valid) and 'ليس باهل للقضاء' (not qualified).
2
Analyze the example of impotence
Consider the scenario: an impotent man gives his wife a khul', but before the tafriq (separation) is finalized, a non-Muslim ruler intervenes and issues a divorce. The text declares this divorce invalid.
This illustrates that even if the parties agree, the non-Muslim's judgment has no effect.
3
Examine the alternative procedures
The text then outlines two valid ways for testimony to be transmitted between courts: via a trustworthy messenger or through a strictly regulated letter (kitāb al-qāḍī ilā al-qāḍī). Any other method is invalid.
This reinforces that only competent Muslim Qadis can handle Muslim cases.
4
Understand the rationale
Competence (ahlīyah) is a necessary condition for a judge. The judgment of an incompetent person is void. Similarly, testimony before an incompetent judge is inadmissible.
The principle: 'naa ahil ka faisla ghair mu'tabar' (the decision of an unqualified person is not valid).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لیکن اگر کسی جگہ فیصلہ کرنے والا حاکم غیر معتبر ہے اُس کے حکم سے (شرعا، فتح وغیرہ نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ " لان الکافر لیس باهل للقضاء على المسلم كما هو مصرح - في جميع كتب الفقه (یعنی کافر مسلمان کے فیصلے کرنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے جیسا کہ کتب فقہ میں وضاحت ہے)  مقدمہ غیرمسلم قدہ کی کاروائی مسلم کے مر کرنے کا کم گردید کی پوری کاردانی فیلم تریب دے اور مسلمان حاکم اس مقدمہ کا مرتب فیصلہ کرے یا اس کا مکس ہو یعنی کوئی مسلمان، مقدمہ کی پوری کا روائی ترتیب دے اور غیر مسلم فیصلہ دے) جب بھی شرعا اس قسم کا فیصلہ نافذ نہیں ہوگا ۔ نامرد کے عقد میں غیرمسلم کا صلہ کرنا اگر میں نامرد کو سلمان سام نے بہلت دی لیکن تفرق شرعی (علیحدگی سے قبل دوسرا غیرمسلم حاکم آجائے اور اُس نے تفریق واقع کر دی تو وہ تفریق مسیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ جس طریقہ سے فیصلہ کرنے کے لیے فیصلہ کرنے کا اہل ہونا شرط ہے اور نا اہل کا فیصلہ غیر معتبر ہے اسی طریقہ سے نا اہل کے سامنے گواہی ہو یا کوئی دوسرا قاضی جس کے سامنے گواہی پیش ہوئی ہے وہ ضابطہ شرع کے مطابق یعنی کتا القاضی الی القاضی کے ضابطہ سے جو شرائط ہیں اُن کے مطابق تحریر کر کے فیصلہ کرنے والا قاضی کے پاس شرائط کے مطابق پہونچا دے۔ ان دو صورتوں کے علاوہ قاضی کو فیصلہ کرنے کا اختیار نہیں ہے جیسا کہ مندرجہ ذیل فقہی جزئیات سے واضح ہے۔ ولو جاء المدعى من القاضي برسول ثقه مامون عدلي الى قاضي آخره يقبل لانه لا يزيد على ان يأتي القاضي بنفسه ويخبر و هو غير لايته كواحد من الرعاية بخلاف كتابه لأنه كالخطاب من مجلس قضائه الخ نیز بحر الرائی ہی میں سراج الوہاج سے منقول ہے : ولو شهد شهود بحق شومات القاضي المشهود عنده و ولى قاضى أخر لو ينفذ تلك الشهادة حتى تعاد ألخ . (بحر الرائق م ج ع )
But if the ruler making the decision in a place is not trustworthy, his judgment (shar'ian) cannot be effective. Because the disbeliever is not qualified to judge the Muslim, as is clearly stated in all books of fiqh (i.e., a disbeliever is not authorized to judge a Muslim). A case of a non-Muslim: If the proceedings of a case are organized by a Muslim, and a Muslim governor gives the final judgment, or if it is mixed: a Muslim organizes the entire proceedings and a non-Muslim gives the judgment, then such a judgment is not effective. The reconciliation of an impotent man by a non-Muslim: If an impotent man gives a woman a khul' but before separation (tafriq shar'i) another non-Muslim ruler comes and effects a separation, that separation is not valid. Because just as for a judgment, competence is required and the judgment of an incompetent is invalid, similarly testimony before an incompetent is invalid. Or if another Qadi before whom testimony was presented writes it according to Sharia procedure (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi) and sends it to the judge, with all conditions met, then the judge may rule. Other than these two ways, a Qadi has no authority to judge, as is clear from the following fiqhi details: 'If the plaintiff comes from a judge with a trustworthy, reliable, just messenger to another judge, it is accepted because it is no more than if the judge came himself and informed, and he is not in his jurisdiction like one of the people, unlike his writing because it is like an address from his court.' Also in Bahr al-Raiq from Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'If witnesses testify to a right before a judge, and that judge dies and another judge is appointed, he cannot implement that testimony unless it is given again.' (Bahr al-Raiq).
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) addressing the incompetence of non-Muslim judges in cases involving Muslims. The author states plainly that a non-Muslim cannot judge a Muslim, citing consensus in fiqh works. Then he gives two scenarios: (1) If a Muslim organizes the case but a non-Muslim gives the verdict, the verdict is void. (2) In a case of impotence (khul'), if a non-Muslim ruler intervenes and grants a divorce before the proper Shari'a separation, it is not valid. The author then explains the proper procedure for transmitting testimony from one Qadi to another via a messenger (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi) or by sending a written letter, and that testimony must be re-given if the first Qadi dies. This reinforces the rule that only a competent Muslim judge can handle Muslim matters.

Key Terms
نامرد (namard): An impotent man, i.e., a husband who cannot engage in sexual intercourse.
کتاب القاضی الی القاضی (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A written communication from one Qadi to another regarding a case, containing testimony or a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The divorce is invalid and not recognized
D) The divorce is valid only if the couple agrees

Key Takeaway
A non-Muslim judge's ruling regarding a Muslim is fundamentally invalid and has no legal effect in Islamic law.

The Deputy Qadi and Judicial Delegation
Explains the ruling on deputy qadi delegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for valid delegation to a deputy qadi and apply the principle to resolve conflicts between the qadi and his deputy.

Core Idea
Delegation to a deputy must be for judgment (qada), not just hearing (sama).

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on appointing a deputy qadi. The key distinction is whether the principal delegated the power to judge or merely to hear testimony. Understanding this impacts the validity of the deputy's decisions and how contradictions with the original qadi are resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation types
The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes two types of delegation: for qada (judgment) and for sama (hearing). Determine which type produces valid decisions.
The original qadi's statement takes precedence in case of conflict.
2
Match delegation type to effect
If delegated for qada, the deputy's decisions are valid. If only for sama, they carry no weight.
3
Apply to resolve contradictions
When the original qadi and deputy differ, the original's view prevails, as he has full authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر کسی خاتون و غیره نائب قاضی کے مقدمہ کی کاروائی ترتیب دینے کی حیثیت سے مقدم کی نائب قاضی کا روائی مرتب کرے تو اس میں تفصیل ہے جیسا کہ کرالائق ہیں میں مٹ جے ہیں ہے : ما نصه صدر الشهيد النائب يقضى بما شهد وعند الاصل و كذا الأصل ويقضى بما شهد وعند النائب الخ نیز بعض جزئیات نقل کرنے کے بعد ہے ۔ تخالف بعضها بعضا فالى صل أن القاضي اذا ولى الخليفة القضاء عمل بقوله وان ولأو سماع الدعوى والشهادة لا يعمل فلا تناقض الخ بحر الرائق مٹ ج اگر کسی مقدمہ کا فیصلہ کسی کیٹی کے سپرد بورڈ یاکمیٹی کے فیصلہ کی حیثیت کر دیا جائے جیسا کہ بعض مرتبہ کسی عدالت کے جموں کی جیوری کے سپرد ہوتا ہے یا پنچ کے سامنے پیش ہوتا ہے یا کوئی مقدمہ چند حضرات کی کمیٹی کے سپرد ہوتا ہے تو اس صورت میں ان تمام ارکان کا مسلمان ہونا شرط ہے کوئی غیر مسلم حج اور مجسٹریٹ اور مبر بھی اس کا رکن ہو تو شرعاً اس جماعت کا فیصلہ کسی طرح معتبر نہیں ، اس قسم کے فیصلہ سے تفریق (علیمی گیا) وغیرہ ہرگز صحیح نہیں ہوگی۔ جس جگہ مسلمان حاکم موجود نہ ہو یا سلمان حاکم اگر مسلمان مجسٹریٹ نہ مل سکے ؟ کی عدالت میں مقدمہ لے جانے کا قانونا اختیار لے لیکن یہ صورت طلاق وغیرہ کی جب ہوسکتی ہے جبکہ شوہر عاقل و بالغ ہوادر مفقود (بالکل لاپتہ ) نہ ہو کیونکہ مجنون اور نابالغ سے طلاق وغیرہ صحیح نہیں ہے اور مفقود سے طلاق لینے کی صورت نہیں ۔

نہ ہو یا مسلمان حاکم شرعی قانون کے مطابق فیصلہ نہ کرتا ہو تو ایسی صورت میں فقہ حنفی کے اعتبار سے تو عورت کی علیحد گی کے لیے شوہر کی طلاق وغیرہ کے بغیر کوئی صوت نہیں ہے
If a lady etc. appoints a deputy qadi to arrange proceedings of a case, and the deputy qadi arranges the proceedings, then there is detail in it, as is in Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 'The text: The chief witness, the deputy judges by what he witnesses, and according to the original, likewise. And the original judges by what he witnesses, and according to the deputy, etc.' Also after quoting some details: 'If some contradict others, then the principle is that if the qadi appoints a deputy for judgment, his statement is acted upon, and if he appoints for hearing the claim and testimony, it is not acted upon, so there is no contradiction.' (Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) If a case is referred to a committee or board for decision, as sometimes occurs in a court's jury or is presented before a panchayat, or a case is referred to a committee of several persons, then in that case it is a condition that all members be Muslim. If any non-Muslim, judge, magistrate, or member is also a member, then that group's decision is not valid in any way. A separation (talaq) etc. based on such a decision is never valid. Where a Muslim ruler is not present, or if a Muslim ruler takes legal permission to take the case to a non-Muslim court because a Muslim magistrate is not available, then this situation for talaq etc. is possible only when the husband is sane and adult and not missing (completely lost), because talaq from an insane or minor is not valid, and there is no way to obtain talaq from a missing person. Or if a Muslim ruler does not decide according to Sharia law, then according to Hanafi fiqh, there is no way for the woman's separation without the husband's talaq etc.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from Hanafi fiqh regarding the validity of judicial decisions. First, it discusses the deputy qadi (na'ib qadi): if a deputy is appointed to arrange proceedings, his ruling is based on his own testimony, but if there is conflict between the original qadi and the deputy, the original qadi's statement prevails, as quoted from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. This is a rasm rule about delegation of judicial authority. Second, it addresses situations where a case is referred to a committee, board, or panchayat: all members must be Muslim. If any non-Muslim is a member (whether as judge, magistrate, or ordinary member), the decision is completely invalid in Sharia, including any talaq based on it. Third, it considers scenarios where a Muslim judge is absent or does not rule according to Sharia. In such cases, Hanafi fiqh allows taking the case to a non-Muslim court only if the husband is sane, adult, and not missing. If the husband is insane, minor, or missing, talaq from such a person is not valid. If a Muslim judge rules contrary to Sharia, there is no way for a woman to obtain separation without the husband's talaq. These rules guide the mufti when faced with complex situations involving non-Muslim courts or invalid judicial bodies.

Key Terms
نائب قاضی (na'ib qadi): A deputy appointed by the qadi to perform certain judicial functions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if the qadi appoints a deputy for hearing the claim and testimony, the deputy's judgment is:
A) Valid
B) Not valid
C) Valid only if the qadi confirms
D) Valid for non-Muslims

Key Takeaway
A deputy qadi's decision is valid only if he is delegated to judge, not merely to hear.

Non-Muslim Judges and Panels: When Decisions Are Invalid
Explains the requirement of Islam for judges and its implications for divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that all members of a judicial panel must be Muslim for its decision to be valid in Muslim cases, and explain the conditions under which talaq from a non-Muslim court is permissible.

Core Idea
A judicial decision involving Muslims is invalid if any member is non-Muslim.

This lesson addresses the requirement that judges and arbitrators be Muslim when deciding cases involving Muslims. The passage also covers limited exceptions when a Muslim judge is absent and the husband is sane and present. Understanding this is critical for muftis dealing with marriages and divorces in mixed or non-Muslim legal systems.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for committees/panels
The passage explicitly states that all members must be Muslim. Determine what constitutes a panel (panchayat, jury, committee).
2
Apply to talaq decisions
Any talaq based on a decision from a panel with a non-Muslim member is invalid.
3
Consider exceptions for non-Muslim courts
If no Muslim judge is available, a Muslim may take the case to a non-Muslim court, but only if the husband is sane, adult, and not missing. If he is insane, minor, or missing, no valid talaq is possible.
4
Note the limitation when Muslim judge does not rule by Sharia
If a Muslim ruler does not judge by Sharia, Hanafi fiqh provides no alternative for separation without the husband's talaq.
