In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to Allah and peace upon His chosen servants, especially our master the chosen one (Muhammad) and those who follow his guidance. After praise and salutation: It is stated that difference of religion between spouses is of two types. First: the difference exists before marriage. Second: it arises after marriage. In the first case, the marriage of a Muslim woman to any disbelieving man is not permissible in any case, regardless of the type of disbelief. Similarly, the marriage of a Muslim man to any disbelieving woman is not permissible. However, if the woman is Kitabiyya (i.e., Jewish or Christian, etc.), then a Muslim man's marriage to her can occur with two conditions. First: that she is not merely a nominal Christian or Jew like the common European nations, being in fact irreligious, but that she at least believes in her religious principles, even if she acts contrary. Second: that she was originally Jewish or Christian, not having apostatized from Islam to Judaism or Christianity. When these two conditions are found in a Kitabiyya woman, marriage to her is valid and contracted. But without severe necessity, marrying her is makruh (disliked) and contains many evils. That is why Sayyiduna Umar al-Faruq (may Allah be pleased with him) during his caliphate prohibited Muslims from marrying Kitabiyya women. (Narrated by Hafiz Ibn Kathir in his tafsir of the verse 'And do not marry polytheist women until they believe'; Imam Muhammad in Kitab al-Athar, and Allama al-Shami in Muharramat declared it karaha and in the case of warring women, prohibited.) And when in the Umar's era, which was a better time, such considerations existed, then today...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage lays down the foundational ruling on marriage between Muslims and non-Muslims. It distinguishes two scenarios: religious difference pre-existing before marriage, and arising after marriage. In the first scenario, a Muslim woman can never marry a non-Muslim man, regardless of his type of disbelief. A Muslim man cannot marry a non-Muslim woman except if she is a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian) and meets two conditions: (1) she genuinely believes in her religion (not merely nominal), and (2) she was originally from that religion, not an apostate from Islam. If both conditions are met, the marriage is valid but makruh (disliked) without necessity. The passage cites the practice of Caliph Umar (RA) who prohibited such marriages during his caliphate due to practical harms. The text thus reinforces that while technically permissible, marrying a Kitabiyya is discouraged and should be avoided except when necessary.

Key Terms
کتابیہ (kitabiyya): Woman from the People of the Book (Jewish or Christian)
انعقاد (in'aqad): Contracting of marriage validly
مکروہ (makruh): Disliked but not prohibited

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid with conditions
B) Not valid because she is not originally Kitabiyya
C) Permissible but makruh
D) Prohibited in all cases

Key Takeaway
A marriage to a Kitabiyya is valid only if she genuinely believes and is originally from that faith, and even then it is disliked without necessity.

Ruling on Apostasy of Spouses: Distinction Between Husband and Wife
Ruling on apostasy of spouse and correction of common misunderstanding regarding wife's apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on apostasy of husband vs wife, and identify the mufta bihi position for the wife's apostasy based on sadd al-dhara'i.

Core Idea
The husband's apostasy dissolves marriage instantly; the wife's apostasy does not, to block her trick.

This passage addresses a critical distinction in Hanafi family law: the apostasy of a husband versus a wife. While the husband's apostasy dissolves the marriage by consensus, the wife's apostasy does not lead to automatic dissolution, contrary to a common misunderstanding. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, clarifies the fatwa hierarchy and warns against misapplication that could lead to serious sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct cases
Recognize that the passage treats husband's apostasy and wife's apostasy separately. The rulings are fundamentally different.
The author emphasizes that the rulings are 'separate and as follows'.
2
Analyze the ruling on husband's apostasy
Note the consensus of the four Imams that the marriage dissolves automatically (faskh) without need for a judge. Identify the financial consequences depending on whether valid seclusion occurred.
The passage quotes al-Durr al-Mukhtar and al-Alamgiriyya to confirm immediate separation.
3
Recognize the common misunderstanding and its harm
Some people wrongly apply the husband's ruling to the wife, believing she can remarry after renewing Islam. The author condemns this as a dangerous trick that leads to apostasy.
The author calls it 'the greatest of major sins' and says the objective cannot be achieved legally.
4
Navigate the ikhtilaf on wife's apostasy
Understand that Zahir al-Riwayah says apostasy dissolves marriage for both. However, later scholars of Balkh and Samarqand gave fatwa that it does not, to block the trick. Scholars of Bukhara held that it dissolves but she is forced to return.
This is a classic case of adjusting fatwa based on maqasid (sadd al-dhara'i).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author clearly supports the Balkh/Samarqand view: the wife's apostasy does not dissolve the marriage. She must renew Islam and renew marriage with her original husband.
This is the relied-upon position for fatwa, despite the apparent madhhab text.

اذا ارتد احد الزوجين عن الاسلام وقعت الفرقة بغير طلاق في الحال قبل الدخول وبعده -

حکم ارتداد زوجه

زوجہ کے ارتداد میں روایات مختلف ہیں اور کسی قدر تفصیل ہے جو ذیل بحوالہ کتب درج ہے۔
را) في الهداية من باب النكاح الكافر - اذا ارتد احد الزوجين وقعت الفرقة بغير طلاق انتى قال المحقق ابن الهمام هذا جواب ظاهر المذهب وبعض مشائخ بلخ و سمرقند افتوا في ردتها بعدم الفرقة حتما لاحتيالها على الخلاص باكبر الكبائر وعامة مشائخ بخارا افتوا بالفرقة وجبرها على الاسلام وعلى النكاح
The second is the apostasy of the wife. The rulings of both are separate and as follows, and the rulings of these possibilities with the verified signatures of all scholars are recorded. ... So: In the rulings of the first case of difference in religion between spouses and in the rulings of three of the four possibilities of the second case, there was no obscurity or disagreement. Therefore, their draft was not presented before all scholars; rather, only Hakim al-Ummat damat barakatuhum and a few scholars' review sufficed. And in the rulings of some possibilities there was some obscurity and disagreement, so only the rulings of those possibilities were presented and the signatures of all scholars were obtained.

Ruling on Apostasy of Husband:
If, God forbid, a woman's husband apostatizes from Islam and becomes an apostate, then by consensus of the four Imams and agreement of the majority of jurists, his marriage is automatically dissolved, without need for a judge's verdict or ruler's order. If this apostasy occurred before valid seclusion (khalwat sahiha), then half the dower is owed by the husband, and iddah is not obligatory upon the wife. If apostasy occurred after valid seclusion, then the full dower is obligatory, and iddah is also obligatory upon the wife. Also, the apostate is liable for maintenance during iddah, as is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar... and in Radd al-Muhtar...

And in al-Alamgiriyya, Kitab al-Nikah, Bab al-Nikah, Chapter 10, page 317, volume one (Egyptian) it says: 'When one of the spouses apostatizes from Islam, separation occurs without divorce immediately, both before and after consummation.'

Some people, seeing only these phrases, understood from mere apostasy the absolute dissolution of marriage, thinking that if a wife apostatizes, then the marriage will also be dissolved. Based on this, out of sheer ignorance and contrary to all juristic transmissions, they played around, stating that this unworthy woman, after renewing Islam, is permitted to marry another husband. So much so that some ill-fated women considered this an easy remedy to get rid of their husbands, and, falling into the great calamity of apostasy, ruined their lifetime of good deeds, whereas legally their objective still cannot be achieved. Because in this case, marriage to another person is absolutely not permitted. Rather, it is necessary that after renewing Islam and renewing the marriage, she remain with her first husband, the details of which will shortly come in the discussion of the wife's apostasy.

Ruling on Apostasy of Wife:
Regarding the wife's apostasy, the transmissions differ, and there is some detail, which is recorded below with reference to books. It is narrated in al-Hidayah, from the chapter on marriage of the disbeliever: 'When one of the spouses apostatizes, separation occurs without divorce.' [Ibn al-Humam said: This is the apparent madhhab. And some scholars of Balkh and Samarqand gave fatwa regarding her apostasy that separation does not occur at all, to prevent her from using the greatest of major sins as a trick to get free. And the general scholars of Bukhara gave fatwa of separation and forcing her to Islam and to the marriage.]
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on apostasy of spouses, with clear distinction between husband and wife. The author opens by noting that there are two cases (husband and wife) and that the rulings are separate. He then gives the ruling for the husband's apostasy: by consensus of all four Imams, the marriage is automatically dissolved (faskh) without any need for a judge's decree. This applies whether before or after consummation, but the financial consequences differ: if before khalwat, only half mahr is due and no iddah; if after, full mahr and iddah are due, with the apostate husband liable for maintenance during iddah. The author quotes al-Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar to confirm that no judicial declaration is needed.

The passage then warns against a grave misunderstanding: some people, seeing general statements like 'apostasy dissolves marriage immediately,' mistakenly apply the same ruling to a wife's apostasy, allowing her to remarry after renewing Islam. The author strongly condemns this, noting that some women even used apostasy as a trick to escape their husbands. He clarifies that in the wife's case, the marriage does not dissolve to permit remarriage; rather, she must renew Islam and renew the marriage with her original husband.

Finally, the author presents the actual dispute regarding the wife's apostasy. He quotes from al-Hidayah that the apparent madhhab is that apostasy dissolves the marriage. However, he then cites a significant ikhtilaf: the scholars of Balkh and Samarqand gave fatwa that separation does not occur at all, in order to block the trick (hila) of using apostasy to escape marriage. The general scholars of Bukhara gave fatwa that separation does occur, but the woman is compelled to return to Islam and to the marriage. This reflects the Hanafi principle that when a ruling leads to misuse, scholars may adjust the fatwa to prevent harm.

Key Terms
ارتداد (irtidād): Apostasy; a Muslim's renunciation of Islam. In this text, it specifically refers to the act of a spouse leaving Islam.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or dissolution of a contract; here, the automatic termination of the marriage contract due to apostasy.
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwat ṣaḥīḥa): Valid seclusion between husband and wife in a place where intercourse is not physically or legally prevented.
سد ذرائع (sadd al-dhara'i): Blocking the means to evil; a legal principle used to prevent actions that could lead to haram.
ظاهر المذهب (zahir al-madhhab): The apparent or transmitted position of the school, as recorded in the core texts (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the text, what happens to the marriage when a wife apostatizes and then returns to Islam?
A) The marriage is dissolved and she may remarry anyone.
B) The marriage is not dissolved; she must return to her original husband after renewing Islam and renewing the marriage.
C) The marriage is dissolved but she must remain single for a year.
D) The marriage is automatically renewed upon her return to Islam.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on apostasy of spouses is not uniform: the husband's apostasy dissolves the marriage, but the wife's apostasy does not, to block her from using it as a trick.

Three Hanafi Opinions on Apostate Woman's Marriage
Debate on marriage dissolution upon apostasy with three opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions regarding the dissolution of marriage upon a woman's apostasy and identify the operative position (mufta bihi) based on the fatwa hierarchy.

Core Idea
A woman's apostasy triggers a dispute: one view dissolves marriage, another keeps it intact; the mufta bihi may depend on regional practice.

This lesson explores the intra-Hanafi disagreement on the effect of a woman's apostasy on her marriage. Three positions are recorded, with the scholars of Balkh and Samarkand arguing against the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah). Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for issuing fatwas in different contexts, as the practical consequences vary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
List the three Hanafi positions on the marriage of an apostate woman: (1) Zahir al-Riwayah: marriage dissolves but she is forced to remarry her husband; (2) Scholars of Balkh/Samarkand: marriage does not dissolve; (3) a third opinion not fully detailed in this passage but alluded to.
The third opinion may be that marriage dissolves with no forced remarriage; check other sources.
2
Understand the evidence for each opinion
For Zahir al-Riwayah, the analogy with original disbelief and punishment; for Balkh, contextual limitation of the general text and the specific parts of the verse.
The Balkh opinion relies on qara'in (contextual clues) that indicate the verse's rulings do not apply to apostates.
3
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Zahir al-Riwayah, the woman is forced to renew marriage with her former husband; under Balkh, she remains married without interruption.
4
Evaluate the tarjih
The author of this passage seems to support the Balkh position, calling it 'the verification of the earlier scholars.' Later Hanafi jurists often adopted this view in practice.
However, Zahir al-Riwayah remains the official doctrine in some regions.

بیوی کے مرتد ہونے کی صورت میں تین قول کی تفصیل روایات مذکورہ بال سے کے برتر ہونے کی صورت میں مذہب حنفیہ میں تین قول ہیں ۔ یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ عورت ایک ظاہر الروایہ جس کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ عورت کے مرتد ہو نے سے نکاح تو فسخ ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن پھر اس کو حبس و قید کر کے تجدید اسلام پر بھی اور اس پر بھی مجبور کیا جائے گا کہ وہ اپنے پہلے ہی خاوند سے تجدید نکاح کرنے جیسا کہ عبارت ا قاضی خان نمبر اور عالمگیری کی جارت نمبر اور عبارت در مختار و شامی نمبر میں اس کی تصریح ہے کہ ظاہر الہدایہ جس میں فسخ نکاح کا حکم دیا گیا ہے اُس کے ساتھ یہ بھی مذکور ہے کہ عورت کو تجدید اسلام اور شوہر اول سے تجدید نکاح پر حکومت مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ خواہ اس کے مرتد ہونے کی غرض خاوند اول سے علیدہ ہونا ہی ہو یا حقیقہ اُس کے عقائد بدل گئے ہوں دونوں صورتوں میں اس کو تجدید نکاح پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ كما صرح به الشامي حيث قال ولا يلزم من هذا ان يكون الجبر على تجديد النكاح مقصورا على ما اذا ارتدت لأجل الخلاص منه بل قالوا ذلك سدا لهذا الباب من اصله سواء تعمدت المجلة ام لا كيلا تجعل ذلك حيلة (شامي باب النكاح الكافومت با دو سرا قول مشائخ بلخ وسمر قند اور ) بعض مشائخ بخارا ابو سهل زاہد اور ابو النصر الدبوہی اور ابو القاسم صفار کا فتویٰ ہے کہ عورت کے مرتد ہونے کی صورت میں نکاح فسخ ہی نہیں ہوتا، بلکہ بدستور یہ عورت شوہر
If the dissolution of marriage by apostasy was the intended meaning of the text, then it is not hidden from the likes of the great Imams, and therefore one does not find in any book that those who chose the apparent transmission deny the scholars of Balkh and Samarkand on the grounds of contradicting the text. For if their fatwa were contradictory to the text, they would have criticized it and rejected it completely. And if it is said that the text of the verse and its indication, even if it does not include our case, but it is annexed to it through textual analogy, we say: that is what we sought. For it is established that ijtihad regarding it is equal to the text in the one who did not annex it to the specific text due to a difference between them. The cause for dissolution in the specific text is refusing Islam or remaining on disbelief as a punishment for his action. There is no doubt that apostasy after Islam is more severe and stronger than remaining on original disbelief and refusing Islam, so it requires a stronger punishment. How can the stronger be punished with the lighter? And no doubt that the ruling of dissolution of marriage for the apostate woman with her choice of husbands and leaving her free so that she goes where she wishes and marries whom she wishes, like the original disbeliever, is not from punishment and obligations in anything, rather it is exactly her desire and aim. We seek refuge in Allah from ruling dissolution on the basis of punishment for what she earned from her apostasy; it is more severe and closer to deterrence, and she is more deserving of it. This is the gist of the verification of the earlier scholars. I say: If there is doubt in your heart that consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause, and the wording is general and includes our case, and it contradicts the fatwa of the scholars of Balkh, they respond that what is intended by this principle is the generality that does not exceed the speaker's intention understood from contextual clues, not absolute generality. The necessary consequence would be to say prohibition of fasting while traveling absolutely due to the hadith 'It is not from righteousness to fast while traveling', but that consequence is negated. Here, the speaker does not intend generality for our case. Its proof is the very parts of the verse: 'And ask for what you have spent' and 'pay back what they spent' and 'And if any of your wives have gone away from you, then you have given them their dowries, then those who have gone away, give them the like of what they spent.' These rulings are not general for our case, and consensus indicates lack of generality as mentioned by Ibn al-Arabi. Now the Urdu: A detailed explanation of three opinions in the case of a woman's apostasy. From the above-mentioned narrations, it is established that in the Hanafi school there are three opinions. The first is the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) whose summary is that the marriage is dissolved by the woman's apostasy. But then she is imprisoned and forced to renew Islam and also forced to renew marriage with her previous husband, as stated in the expressions of Qadikhan, Alamgiri, Durr al-Mukhtar, and Shami: that in Zahir al-Hidayah where the dissolution is ruled, it is also mentioned that the government will force her to renew Islam and remarry the first husband, whether her apostasy was intended to separate from her husband or her beliefs actually changed. As stated by al-Shami: 'It does not follow from this that compulsion to renew marriage is limited to when she apostatizes to get rid of him. Rather, they said it to close this door from its root, whether she intended the trick or not, so that it not be made a legal stratagem.' The second opinion is that of the Mashayikh of Balkh, Samarkand, and some Mashayikh of Bukhara, Abu Sahl al-Zahid, Abu al-Nasr al-Dabusi, and Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar: that in case of a woman's apostasy, the marriage is not dissolved at all; rather, the woman remains under her husband.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the Hanafi positions on the effect of a woman's apostasy on her marriage, and it serves as a case study in usul al-iftaa regarding the interpretation of general texts. The author first presents the argument that the dissolution of marriage upon apostasy, even if not explicitly mentioned in the Quranic verse (Mumtahanah 60:10) about disbelieving women, can be annexed by analogy because apostasy is a stronger offense than original disbelief. Then he shifts to the principle of 'al-ibratu li 'umum al-lafz la li khusus al-sabab' (consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the reason). The scholars of Balkh and Samarkand opposed the Zahir al-Riwayah position that the marriage is dissolved, arguing that the general wording of the verse is not meant to be absolutely general, but is qualified by contextual clues (qara'in). They draw an analogy with the hadith about fasting while traveling, which is not taken as an absolute prohibition. The passage then mentions three opinions in the Hanafi school: (1) the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah): marriage dissolves but she is forced to renew Islam and remarry her former husband, to prevent trickery; (2) the position of the Mashayikh of Balkh, Samarkand, and some of Bukhara: marriage does not dissolve at all; the third opinion is not fully stated here but is alluded to. The author supports the second position by citing the verse's parts that indicate specificity. This lesson is important for understanding how Hanafi jurists navigate between textual generality and contextual limitation, a key rasm rule for issuing fatwas.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The most widely transmitted and authoritative opinions of the Hanafi school, primarily from Imam Muhammad's six books.
المرتدة (al-murtaddah): A woman who has apostatized from Islam to disbelief.
العبرة لعموم اللفظ لا لخصوص السبب (al-ibratu li 'umum al-lafz la li khusus al-sabab): A principle in usul al-fiqh stating that when interpreting a legal text, the general wording is applied, not the specific event that prompted its revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The marriage is dissolved and she is forced to remarry her former husband.
B) The marriage is not dissolved; she remains married to her husband.
C) She is executed for apostasy.
D) She is free to marry anyone she chooses.

Key Takeaway
The fatwa on an apostate woman's marriage depends on whether the general text is limited by contextual clues; the mufti must know which opinion is operative in his region.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
10 min
Limiting General Texts by Contextual Clues
Application of usul principle to a specific fiqh case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that general wording is limited by contextual clues (qara'in) and distinguish it from absolute generality.

Core Idea
General wording is not always universal; contextual clues can restrict its scope to specific cases.

This lesson examines the Hanafi usul principle that 'consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the reason' (al-ibratu li 'umum al-lafz la li khusus al-sabab). However, Hanafis qualify this principle: the generality does not exceed the speaker's intention understood from contextual clues (qara'in). The case of the apostate woman's marriage provides a concrete application.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its standard application
Recite the principle: 'al-ibratu li 'umum al-lafz la li khusus al-sabab'. Normally, a general text is applied to all its instances regardless of the specific reason for its revelation.
2
Explain the Hanafi caveat
Hanafis limit this principle: the generality is not absolute; it is confined by contextual clues (qara'in) that indicate the speaker's intended scope. This is a key distinguishing feature of Hanafi usul.
The dabt (sub-restriction) of this qawida is: 'العموم الذي لا يجاوز مداد المتكلم المفهوم من القرائن'.
3
Identify the qara'in in the verse (Mumtahanah 60:10)
The scholars of Balkh pointed to specific parts of the verse (asking for dowries, paying back dowries) that do not apply to an apostate woman. These indicate that the general term 'disbelieving women' was not intended to include apostates.
The verse says: 'And ask for what you have spent' and 'pay back what they spent' – these rulings presuppose that the women are leaving their Muslim husbands, which is not the case for an apostate woman who stays with her husband.
4
Compare with the hadith on fasting while traveling
The hadith 'ليس من البر الصيام في السفر' is a general prohibition of fasting while traveling, but it is not taken absolutely; it is limited by context (e.g., if fasting does not harm). This analogy supports the Balkh interpretation.
5
Derive the ruling for the apostate woman
Since the verse's generality is limited by qara'in, the apostate woman is not included. Therefore, her marriage does not dissolve upon apostasy.
This is the ruling of the Balkh scholars and the mufta bihi in many later Hanafi works.

Source Text

نعوذ بالله الحكم بفسخ النكاح على وجه المعاقبة جزاءً بما اكتسبت من ارتدادها اغلظ واقرب للانزجار وهي اولى به انتهى هذا محصل تحقيق علماء السلف نقول مد فيو فيهو العالية + قلت فان زال في قلبك ان العبرة لعموم اللفظ لا التخصيص السبب واللفظ عام فيشمل ما نحن فيه ويخالفه فتوى علماء بلخ ناحه بان المراد في هذا الأصل العموم الذي لا يجاوز مداد المتكلم المفهوم من القرائن لا العموم المطلق واللازم القول بالنهي عن الصوم في السفر مطلقا لحديث ليس من البر الصيام في السفر - واللازم منتف وههنا ليس مراد المتكلم العموم لما نحن فيه - ودليله نفس اجزاء الأية من قوله تعالى واسألوا ما انفقتم ويدفعوا ما انفقوا وقوله تعالى وإن فاتكم شيء من ازواجكم فعاتبتم فاتوا الذين ذهبت ازواجهم مثل ما انفقوا فهذه الأحكام ليست عامة لما نحن فيه ويدل على عدم العموم الاجماع لما مر من ابن العربي - بیوی کے مرتد ہونے کی صورت میں تین قول کی تفصیل روایات مذکورہ بال سے کے برتر ہونے کی صورت میں مذہب حنفیہ میں تین قول ہیں ۔ یہ ثابت ہو گیا کہ عورت ایک ظاہر الروایہ جس کا خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ عورت کے مرتد ہو نے سے نکاح تو فسخ ہو جائے گا۔ لیکن پھر اس کو حبس و قید کر کے تجدید اسلام پر بھی اور اس پر بھی مجبور کیا جائے گا کہ وہ اپنے پہلے ہی خاوند سے تجدید نکاح کرنے جیسا کہ عبارت ا قاضی خان نمبر اور عالمگیری کی جارت نمبر اور عبارت در مختار و شامی نمبر میں اس کی تصریح ہے کہ ظاہر الہدایہ جس میں فسخ نکاح کا حکم دیا گیا ہے اُس کے ساتھ یہ بھی مذکور ہے کہ عورت کو تجدید اسلام اور شوہر اول سے تجدید نکاح پر حکومت مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ خواہ اس کے مرتد ہونے کی غرض خاوند اول سے علیدہ ہونا ہی ہو یا حقیقہ اُس کے عقائد بدل گئے ہوں دونوں صورتوں میں اس کو تجدید نکاح پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔ كما صرح به الشامي حيث قال ولا يلزم من هذا ان يكون الجبر على تجديد النكاح مقصورا على ما اذا ارتدت لأجل الخلاص منه بل قالوا ذلك سدا لهذا الباب من اصله سواء تعمدت المجلة ام لا كيلا تجعل ذلك حيلة (شامي باب النكاح الكافومت با دو سرا قول مشائخ بلخ وسمر قند اور ) بعض مشائخ بخارا ابو سهل زاہد اور ابو النصر الدبوہی اور ابو القاسم صفار کا فتویٰ ہے کہ عورت کے مرتد ہونے کی صورت میں نکاح فسخ ہی نہیں ہوتا، بلکہ بدستور یہ عورت شوہر
If the dissolution of marriage by apostasy was the intended meaning of the text, then it is not hidden from the likes of the great Imams, and therefore one does not find in any book that those who chose the apparent transmission deny the scholars of Balkh and Samarkand on the grounds of contradicting the text. For if their fatwa were contradictory to the text, they would have criticized it and rejected it completely. And if it is said that the text of the verse and its indication, even if it does not include our case, but it is annexed to it through textual analogy, we say: that is what we sought. For it is established that ijtihad regarding it is equal to the text in the one who did not annex it to the specific text due to a difference between them. The cause for dissolution in the specific text is refusing Islam or remaining on disbelief as a punishment for his action. There is no doubt that apostasy after Islam is more severe and stronger than remaining on original disbelief and refusing Islam, so it requires a stronger punishment. How can the stronger be punished with the lighter? And no doubt that the ruling of dissolution of marriage for the apostate woman with her choice of husbands and leaving her free so that she goes where she wishes and marries whom she wishes, like the original disbeliever, is not from punishment and obligations in anything, rather it is exactly her desire and aim. We seek refuge in Allah from ruling dissolution on the basis of punishment for what she earned from her apostasy; it is more severe and closer to deterrence, and she is more deserving of it. This is the gist of the verification of the earlier scholars. I say: If there is doubt in your heart that consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause, and the wording is general and includes our case, and it contradicts the fatwa of the scholars of Balkh, they respond that what is intended by this principle is the generality that does not exceed the speaker's intention understood from contextual clues, not absolute generality. The necessary consequence would be to say prohibition of fasting while traveling absolutely due to the hadith 'It is not from righteousness to fast while traveling', but that consequence is negated. Here, the speaker does not intend generality for our case. Its proof is the very parts of the verse: 'And ask for what you have spent' and 'pay back what they spent' and 'And if any of your wives have gone away from you, then you have given them their dowries, then those who have gone away, give them the like of what they spent.' These rulings are not general for our case, and consensus indicates lack of generality as mentioned by Ibn al-Arabi. Now the Urdu: A detailed explanation of three opinions in the case of a woman's apostasy. From the above-mentioned narrations, it is established that in the Hanafi school there are three opinions. The first is the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah) whose summary is that the marriage is dissolved by the woman's apostasy. But then she is imprisoned and forced to renew Islam and also forced to renew marriage with her previous husband, as stated in the expressions of Qadikhan, Alamgiri, Durr al-Mukhtar, and Shami: that in Zahir al-Hidayah where the dissolution is ruled, it is also mentioned that the government will force her to renew Islam and remarry the first husband, whether her apostasy was intended to separate from her husband or her beliefs actually changed. As stated by al-Shami: 'It does not follow from this that compulsion to renew marriage is limited to when she apostatizes to get rid of him. Rather, they said it to close this door from its root, whether she intended the trick or not, so that it not be made a legal stratagem.' The second opinion is that of the Mashayikh of Balkh, Samarkand, and some Mashayikh of Bukhara, Abu Sahl al-Zahid, Abu al-Nasr al-Dabusi, and Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar: that in case of a woman's apostasy, the marriage is not dissolved at all; rather, the woman remains under her husband.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the Hanafi positions on the effect of a woman's apostasy on her marriage, and it serves as a case study in usul al-iftaa regarding the interpretation of general texts. The author first presents the argument that the dissolution of marriage upon apostasy, even if not explicitly mentioned in the Quranic verse (Mumtahanah 60:10) about disbelieving women, can be annexed by analogy because apostasy is a stronger offense than original disbelief. Then he shifts to the principle of 'al-ibratu li 'umum al-lafz la li khusus al-sabab' (consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the reason). The scholars of Balkh and Samarkand opposed the Zahir al-Riwayah position that the marriage is dissolved, arguing that the general wording of the verse is not meant to be absolutely general, but is qualified by contextual clues (qara'in). They draw an analogy with the hadith about fasting while traveling, which is not taken as an absolute prohibition. The passage then mentions three opinions in the Hanafi school: (1) the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah): marriage dissolves but she is forced to renew Islam and remarry her former husband, to prevent trickery; (2) the position of the Mashayikh of Balkh, Samarkand, and some of Bukhara: marriage does not dissolve at all; the third opinion is not fully stated here but is alluded to. The author supports the second position by citing the verse's parts that indicate specificity. This lesson is important for understanding how Hanafi jurists navigate between textual generality and contextual limitation, a key rasm rule for issuing fatwas.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The most widely transmitted and authoritative opinions of the Hanafi school, primarily from Imam Muhammad's six books.
المرتدة (al-murtaddah): A woman who has apostatized from Islam to disbelief.
العبرة لعموم اللفظ لا لخصوص السبب (al-ibratu li 'umum al-lafz la li khusus al-sabab): A principle in usul al-fiqh stating that when interpreting a legal text, the general wording is applied, not the specific event that prompted its revelation.
قرائن (qara'in): Contextual clues that indicate the intended meaning of a text.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars of Balkh, what limits the generality of the verse about disbelieving women?
A) The fact that the verse was revealed after a specific event.
B) Contextual clues (qara'in) from within the verse itself, such as the rulings about dowries.
C) A later verse that abrogates it.
D) The consensus of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of textual interpretation requires looking beyond the literal wording to understand the speaker's intent through contextual clues.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Three Narrations and Their Common Ground
Presentation of three Hanafi narrations on murtaddah's marriage and their shared prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the apostate woman's marriage and identify the common principle agreed upon by all three.

Core Idea
All three narrations agree that the apostate woman cannot marry another man.

This lesson dissects the three distinct positions attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa on the marital status of an apostate woman. Understanding these positions and their shared ruling is essential for any mufti dealing with apostasy cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sayings
From the passage, list the three distinct rulings attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the apostate woman's marriage.
First: marriage dissolved, compulsion to renew. Second: marriage remains. Third: woman enslaved.
2
Note the differences
Observe how each saying differs on the immediate effect of apostasy and the future of the relationship.
First requires dissolution and renewal; second requires nothing; third transforms her legal status.
3
Spot the common ground
Find the one principle that all three sayings share regarding the woman's marital options.
All three prohibit her from marrying another man.
4
Recognize the authority
Classify each saying as Zahir al-Riwayah or Nawadir and understand the hierarchy.
First is Zahir al-Riwayah; the others are Nawadir.

میں بحالت موجودہ غیر ممکن ہو گیا کیونکہ اس کے ایک جزو پر عمل کرنا اگر چہ اختیار میں ہے لیکن دوسرا جزو یعنی تجد ید اسلام اور تجدید نکاح پر مجبور کرنا قطعا اختیار میں نہیں اور نوادر کی روایت پر عمل کرنا عبارت نقل کی گئی ہے لے لیکن اس روایت پر فتوے دینے کے ساتھ یہ بھی ضروری ہے کہ تجدید اسلام اور تجدید نکاح سے قبل شوہر کو استمتاع یعنی صحبت وغیرہ کی اجازت نہ دی جائے جیسا کہ متن میں بھی بعض مسائل ضروریہ کے زیرعنوان عنقریب آتا ہے
She remains in the previous marriage, as clarified in the expressions of Fath al-Qadir, Qadi Khan, Durar, Shami, Fiqh Nishkar, and Sharh Fiqh Akbar. The third saying is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that this woman, even in Dar al-Islam, will be kept as a slave, and her husband's possession over her will remain as before. However, in this narration there is detail: if this apostate woman is in Dar al-Islam, her husband will buy her from the Imam of the Muslims for a price, or if the Imam considers her as booty (fay'), he may give her for free. In any case, without the permission of the Imam, it is not permissible for him to take possession of her. And if she is in Dar al-Harb, there is no need for the Imam; rather, when the husband gains possession of her, she becomes his property. There is no need for the permission of the Imam, etc., as clarified in the book 'Imarat Qayr Nash'. The summary is that if a woman becomes an apostate, there are three sayings regarding her marriage: one is that the marriage is dissolved, but after she re-embraces Islam, she will be compelled to renew the marriage, and she will not be given the option to marry elsewhere (and this is Zahir al-Riwayah). The detail of this issue is that if the woman apostatizes and goes to Dar al-Harb, or if she apostatizes while in Dar al-Harb, then Zahir al-Riwayah also agrees on making her a slave. The difference between Zahir al-Riwayah and Nawadir is only regarding whether she can be made a slave while staying in Dar al-Islam, as is clear from the quoted expressions of Fath al-Qadir and Tanbih. [12 misr?] And mention what we have passed in the margin on the expression of Fanniya that the rules forbid the enslavement of free persons in Dar al-Islam upon capture. Second: the marriage is not dissolved; rather, both remain as husband and wife. Third: the woman will be kept as a slave. Although there is some difference among these three sayings, they all agree on this point: the woman will not be given any right to separate from her first husband's marriage and marry elsewhere. Hence, it is agreed that the woman will have no option at all to marry elsewhere. Now in India in the present situation, to act upon this agreed ruling by choosing the first narration is impossible, because after giving the ruling of dissolution of marriage, there is no power with the Muslims to compel her to renew the marriage. And even where it exists, there are difficulties, as stated in the quoted expression of Shami in the [tambar?]. Therefore, acting upon the first saying, i.e. Zahir al-Riwayah, has become impossible in present-day India because although one part of it (the dissolution) is within our ability, the other part, i.e. compelling her to re-embrace Islam and renew the marriage, is absolutely not within our ability. And acting upon the narration of Nawadir is even more difficult than Zahir al-Riwayah, rather impossible in the present situation. Hence, now there is no option but to adopt the saying of the Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand and give fatwa accordingly. And the author of Nahr, although he did not face the difficulties that we are facing today, in his own time he advised giving fatwa on that narration and considered acting against it as putting into severe difficulty, as quoted in the expression of Shami in [Umar?]. But along with giving fatwa on this narration, it is also necessary that before the renewal of Islam and renewal of marriage, the husband is not permitted to have intercourse (sexual relations), etc., as will soon come in the text under the heading of 'Some Necessary Issues'.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex fiqh ruling concerning an apostate woman (murtaddah) and the fatwa methodology for choosing among multiple narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, presents three distinct positions (aqwal) attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the marital status and legal disposition of a woman who leaves Islam. The first position, which is Zahir al-Riwayah (the well-known transmitted position), holds that the marriage is dissolved immediately upon apostasy. However, if she later returns to Islam, she is compelled to renew the marriage contract with her former husband; she cannot marry anyone else. The second position states that the marriage is not dissolved at all; the couple remains married. The third position, a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that the apostate woman can be enslaved, even if she remains in Dar al-Islam, but with conditions: if in Dar al-Islam, the husband must purchase her from the Muslim ruler (Imam) or receive her as a gift if the ruler considers her part of the spoils; if in Dar al-Harb, the husband may take possession without the ruler's permission. Importantly, all three positions agree on one point: the woman is never given the right to leave her former husband and marry another man. This consensus is crucial. The author then explains the practical application in British India. The first position (Zahir al-Riwayah) cannot be fully implemented because while a qadi can pronounce dissolution, there is no Islamic authority to compel her to re-embrace Islam and renew the marriage. The second position (no dissolution) is not practical. The third position (enslavement) — which was chosen by the later scholars (Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand) — becomes the only viable option for fatwa, even though it is not the Zahir al-Riwayah. However, the author imposes a condition: as long as the woman remains an apostate and has not renewed her Islam and marriage, the husband cannot have marital relations with her. This lesson illustrates the principle of 'al-hila al-najizah' (the effective legal stratagem) in mufti practice: when acting on the primary ruling becomes impossible due to changed circumstances, the mufti may shift to a weaker but executable narration to uphold the spirit of the Shariah.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known and widely transmitted ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa, primarily through the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
نوادر (Nawadir): Rare or less widely transmitted narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa, often from sources other than the six core books.
مشایخ بلخ وسمرقند (Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand): Later Hanafi jurists from the regions of Balkh and Samarkand who often preferred narrations other than Zahir al-Riwayah based on practical considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is the agreed-upon principle among the three narrations regarding the apostate woman?
A) The marriage is always dissolved.
B) The woman can marry any Muslim man after apostasy.
C) The woman cannot marry another man besides her first husband.
D) The woman becomes a slave without conditions.

Key Takeaway
The common principle in all Hanafi narrations is that the apostate woman may not remarry another man, and this consensus guides the mufti's choice.

Fatwa Hierarchy and the Choice of Narration in Changed Circumstances
Fatwa methodology: choosing a weaker narration due to impossibility of implementing Zahir al-Riwayah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of shifting from Zahir al-Riwayah to Nawadir for fatwa when implementation of the primary ruling is impossible, as demonstrated by the Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand.

Core Idea
When Zahir al-Riwayah cannot be enforced, the mufti may adopt a weaker narration to uphold the Shariah's objective.

This lesson focuses on the fatwa methodology (rasm al-mufti) used by the author to justify adopting the third narration (enslavement) over the more authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah. The key factor is impossibility of implementation due to lack of Islamic authority. This principle is crucial for muftis in non-Muslim contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Zahir al-Riwayah ruling
The first narration is Zahir al-Riwayah: marriage dissolved with compulsion to renew. Note its two components: dissolution and compulsion.
2
Assess feasibility of compulsion
In the context of India, the author states there is no power to compel the woman to re-embrace Islam and renew the marriage. Ask: what part of the ruling cannot be executed?
The compulsion part is impossible.
3
Recognize the shift to Nawadir
The author then considers the Nawadir narrations. The third narration (enslavement) is adopted because it is the only one that can be fully implemented, essentially restricting the woman from leaving her husband.
This shift is endorsed by the Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand.
4
Identify the attached condition
Even when using the third narration, marital relations are prohibited until the woman renews her Islam and marriage. This prevents any intimacy during apostasy.
This condition aligns with the common prohibition of remarriage.

She remains in the previous marriage, as clarified in the expressions of Fath al-Qadir, Qadi Khan, Durar, Shami, Fiqh Nishkar, and Sharh Fiqh Akbar. The third saying is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that this woman, even in Dar al-Islam, will be kept as a slave, and her husband's possession over her will remain as before. However, in this narration there is detail: if this apostate woman is in Dar al-Islam, her husband will buy her from the Imam of the Muslims for a price, or if the Imam considers her as booty (fay'), he may give her for free. In any case, without the permission of the Imam, it is not permissible for him to take possession of her. And if she is in Dar al-Harb, there is no need for the Imam; rather, when the husband gains possession of her, she becomes his property. There is no need for the permission of the Imam, etc., as clarified in the book 'Imarat Qayr Nash'. The summary is that if a woman becomes an apostate, there are three sayings regarding her marriage: one is that the marriage is dissolved, but after she re-embraces Islam, she will be compelled to renew the marriage, and she will not be given the option to marry elsewhere (and this is Zahir al-Riwayah). The detail of this issue is that if the woman apostatizes and goes to Dar al-Harb, or if she apostatizes while in Dar al-Harb, then Zahir al-Riwayah also agrees on making her a slave. The difference between Zahir al-Riwayah and Nawadir is only regarding whether she can be made a slave while staying in Dar al-Islam, as is clear from the quoted expressions of Fath al-Qadir and Tanbih. [12 misr?] And mention what we have passed in the margin on the expression of Fanniya that the rules forbid the enslavement of free persons in Dar al-Islam upon capture. Second: the marriage is not dissolved; rather, both remain as husband and wife. Third: the woman will be kept as a slave. Although there is some difference among these three sayings, they all agree on this point: the woman will not be given any right to separate from her first husband's marriage and marry elsewhere. Hence, it is agreed that the woman will have no option at all to marry elsewhere. Now in India in the present situation, to act upon this agreed ruling by choosing the first narration is impossible, because after giving the ruling of dissolution of marriage, there is no power with the Muslims to compel her to renew the marriage. And even where it exists, there are difficulties, as stated in the quoted expression of Shami in the [tambar?]. Therefore, acting upon the first saying, i.e. Zahir al-Riwayah, has become impossible in present-day India because although one part of it (the dissolution) is within our ability, the other part, i.e. compelling her to re-embrace Islam and renew the marriage, is absolutely not within our ability. And acting upon the narration of Nawadir is even more difficult than Zahir al-Riwayah, rather impossible in the present situation. Hence, now there is no option but to adopt the saying of the Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand and give fatwa accordingly. And the author of Nahr, although he did not face the difficulties that we are facing today, in his own time he advised giving fatwa on that narration and considered acting against it as putting into severe difficulty, as quoted in the expression of Shami in [Umar?]. But along with giving fatwa on this narration, it is also necessary that before the renewal of Islam and renewal of marriage, the husband is not permitted to have intercourse (sexual relations), etc., as will soon come in the text under the heading of 'Some Necessary Issues'.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage addresses the complex fiqh ruling concerning an apostate woman (murtaddah) and the fatwa methodology for choosing among multiple narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, presents three distinct positions (aqwal) attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the marital status and legal disposition of a woman who leaves Islam. The first position, which is Zahir al-Riwayah (the well-known transmitted position), holds that the marriage is dissolved immediately upon apostasy. However, if she later returns to Islam, she is compelled to renew the marriage contract with her former husband; she cannot marry anyone else. The second position states that the marriage is not dissolved at all; the couple remains married. The third position, a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa, holds that the apostate woman can be enslaved, even if she remains in Dar al-Islam, but with conditions: if in Dar al-Islam, the husband must purchase her from the Muslim ruler (Imam) or receive her as a gift if the ruler considers her part of the spoils; if in Dar al-Harb, the husband may take possession without the ruler's permission. Importantly, all three positions agree on one point: the woman is never given the right to leave her former husband and marry another man. This consensus is crucial. The author then explains the practical application in British India. The first position (Zahir al-Riwayah) cannot be fully implemented because while a qadi can pronounce dissolution, there is no Islamic authority to compel her to re-embrace Islam and renew the marriage. The second position (no dissolution) is not practical. The third position (enslavement) — which was chosen by the later scholars (Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand) — becomes the only viable option for fatwa, even though it is not the Zahir al-Riwayah. However, the author imposes a condition: as long as the woman remains an apostate and has not renewed her Islam and marriage, the husband cannot have marital relations with her. This lesson illustrates the principle of 'al-hila al-najizah' (the effective legal stratagem) in mufti practice: when acting on the primary ruling becomes impossible due to changed circumstances, the mufti may shift to a weaker but executable narration to uphold the spirit of the Shariah.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known and widely transmitted ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa, primarily through the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
نوادر (Nawadir): Rare or less widely transmitted narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa, often from sources other than the six core books.
مشایخ بلخ وسمرقند (Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand): Later Hanafi jurists from the regions of Balkh and Samarkand who often preferred narrations other than Zahir al-Riwayah based on practical considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Mashayikh of Balkh and Samarqand adopt the third narration (slavery) for fatwa?
A) Because it is the most authentic narration.
B) Because the first narration cannot be fully implemented due to lack of authority.
C) Because they disliked the first two narrations.
D) Because they wanted to oppress women.

Key Takeaway
A mufti may shift from Zahir al-Riwayah to an implementable Nawadir narration when the primary ruling cannot be enforced, provided it upholds the Shariah's core objective.

Missing Husband in Colonized Lands: Qadis vs. Sovereignty
Distinguishing the legal status of a missing person in dar al-harb from colonized lands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on missing persons to colonized lands by identifying whether Muslim qadis are present and investigation is possible.

Core Idea
The presence of Muslim qadis and possibility of investigation, not political sovereignty, determines the legal status of a territory.

This passage addresses a pressing issue in colonized lands: does the ruling of a missing husband follow dar al-harb or dar al-Islam? The author, building on Hanafi usul, provides a nuanced criterion based on the functioning of Islamic judiciary. Understanding this distinction is essential for fatwa in modern contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two territories
Note the default ruling for a missing husband in dar al-harb (no investigation possible, wife must wait for construction/reconstruction period) and in dar al-Islam (standard rules of mafqud).
Also identify the colonized lands (al-bilad al-musta'mara) as a third category.
2
Extract the effective criterion
Locate the phrase 'وجود قضاة' (presence of qadis) and 'إمكان الكشف' (possibility of investigation). These are the operative criteria that override political sovereignty.
This is a qiyas khafi based on custom and necessity.
3
Parse the exception
Note the distinction between 'البلاد الشركية' (polytheistic Arab lands) where even qadis cannot investigate due to danger, and colonized lands where investigation may be possible.
Shows the criterion is practical ability, not mere presence of qadis.
4
Apply the ruling to a new case
Consider a colonized land with a functioning Muslim judiciary. According to the passage, the wife follows the rules of dar al-Islam: no extended waiting period, and the imam ransoms if captured.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text

وهو المفقود فى ارض الحرب بانه من غاب وانقطع خبر، ولم يمكن الكشف عنه لانه فقد فى ارض الحرب اما البلاد المذكورة وان كان حاکما کا فرا فلا تكون كارض حرب من كل وجه لوجود قضاة فيها ورروتهم وامكان الكشف عنه فانصح بهذا ان حكمها حكم من فقدت ببلاد الاسلام فلا تنتظريدة التعمير واما المسئله الرابعة فيلهم جوابها مما قبلها ايضا هوانه لوفرة بين المفقود فى ارض الاسلام وبين المفقود في البلاد المستعمرة لما قدمنا من وجود قضاة المسلمين فيها ولا تها وامكان الكشف فعلى هذا لا تختص الصورة الثانية المذكورة في مختصر بالمسلمة الكائته في بلاد الاسلام تشمل من كانت والبلاد المستعيرة للكفار لما قدمنا ان المراد بالشركية البلاد العربية التي رويمكن للمسلم الوصول اليها فلا تتمكن القضاة من التفتيش فيها لا مطلق البلاد الكفرية لانها ربما تكون سلمية اوزمية واما القاطمة بالبلاد الشركية الحربية نحكمها هي وزوجها حكم المسلمين فيفديهما الامام من بيت المال ان كان والاقمن ماله بالغاما والا فعلى جميع المسلمين
And [the ruling] of the missing in the land of war is that he is one who disappeared and his news was cut off, and investigation is not possible because he was lost in the land of war. As for the mentioned lands, even if they have a ruler like [؟], they are not like the land of war in every respect because of the presence of qadis there and their [؟] and the possibility of investigating him. So understand from this that its ruling is the ruling of one who is lost in the lands of Islam, so she does not wait [؟] for reconstruction. And as for the fourth issue, its answer is understood from what precedes it as well: that there is a difference between the missing in the land of Islam and the missing in the colonized lands because of what we presented regarding the presence of Muslim qadis there and [؟] and the possibility of investigation. Accordingly, the second scenario mentioned in the Mukhtasar is not specific to the Muslim woman in the land of Islam, but includes whoever is in the colonized lands of the disbelievers, because we previously stated that what is meant by polytheistic lands are the Arab lands that a Muslim can reach, so the qadis are not able to investigate there, not absolute disbelieving lands, because they may perhaps be [؟] or [؟]. And as for the one who resides in polytheistic war lands, we rule that she and her husband are subject to the rulings of Muslims; the imam ransoms them from the treasury if it is available, otherwise from his own wealth up to [؟], otherwise upon all Muslims.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a ruling comparing the legal status of a missing husband (mafqud) in different territories. The author clarifies that although colonized lands may be under non-Muslim political control, if there are Muslim qadis present and investigation is possible, they are treated like Muslim lands. This is a nuanced application of the Hanafi classification of dar al-harb. The key distinction is not simply whether the land is under non-Muslim rule, but whether Islamic legal authorities can function. The passage also notes a difference between Arab polytheistic lands (where Muslims cannot easily access) and other non-Muslim lands where investigation may be possible. The ruling includes that the imam must ransom captured persons from the treasury.

Key Terms
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person who has disappeared and whose whereabouts are unknown, specifically a husband in the context of marital rulings.
أرض الحرب (ard al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim control where Islamic law is not enforced and Muslims are not safe.
البلاد المستعمرة (al-bilad al-musta'mara): Colonized lands where non-Muslims rule but Muslim qadis may still function.
البلاد الشركية (al-bilad al-shirkiyya): Polytheistic lands, often referring to Arab polytheistic territories where Muslims cannot safely enter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Whether the land is Arab or non-Arab
D) Whether the missing person is Muslim

Key Takeaway
The legal status of a territory for missing persons depends on the possibility of Islamic legal investigation, not political sovereignty.

Immediate Divorce for the Wife of a Missing Husband: Conditions and Procedure
Ruling on immediate divorce for the wife of a missing husband due to lack of maintenance and fear of corruption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a Hanafi qadi may grant an immediate divorce to a wife whose husband is missing and apply the procedural ruling regarding the qadi's role.

Core Idea
A wife whose missing husband leaves no maintenance and she fears corruption may obtain divorce immediately, without waiting.

This passage addresses a critical scenario in Islamic family law: a husband is missing and has not provided maintenance, leaving his wife in hardship. The text clarifies that under such circumstances, the wife may seek divorce without delay. We will examine the conditions, the procedure before the qadi, and the difference of opinion on whether the qadi pronounces divorce or authorizes the wife to do so.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering conditions
The wife must have no maintenance and be in extreme need or fear moral corruption. These conditions override the usual four-year waiting period.
2
Understand the procedure
The wife must approach the qadi and prove the absence of maintenance and her need. The type of proof is not specified but must be sufficient.
3
Distinguish the two opinions on the qadi's role
The qadi may either pronounce the divorce himself or order the wife to pronounce it. This is a rasm al-mufti rule indicating procedural flexibility.
4
Recognize the family's lack of authority
The wife's family cannot force her to remarry; only if she is legally incompetent (safihah) does her guardian step in to prevent harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما المسئلة الخامسة فجوابها ان المفقود عنها ولم يترك لها نقة واحتاجت غاية الاحتياج او خافت على نفسها الفساد ان لها التطليق بلاتا جيل كما هو مفهوم الشريط في قول الشيخ خليل في مسئلة المفقود تو جل اربع سنين ان دامت نفقتها وقال شراحه قاطبه فان لمرتد منفقتها أو خشيت الفساد فلها التطليق بلا تاجيل فترفع امرها الى الحاكم ونثبت عدم النفقة وارحتياج بما يثبت به ناماات يطلق الحاكم بنفسه اويامرها با التطليق وهو قول الشيخ خليل فهل يطلق الحاكم اريامرها به قولان واما ارادة اهلها تزويجها فلا عبرة به ما لمتطلب الفراق بنفسها الا ان يكون سفيهة فيقوم وليها مقام مما اذا تحقق لديضرونها
And as for the fifth issue, its answer is that the woman whose husband is missing and he left her no maintenance, and she is in extreme need or fears moral corruption for herself, that she may obtain divorce without delay, as is the understood condition in the statement of Shaykh Khalil in the issue of the missing: 'Delay four years if her maintenance continues.' And his commentators all said: 'If her maintenance is cut off or she fears corruption, she may divorce without delay.' So she raises her matter to the qadi, and proves the absence of maintenance and her need with whatever proves it. [Unclear: namaat] the qadi himself pronounces divorce or orders her to divorce. This is the statement of Shaykh Khalil. There are two opinions: does the qadi himself pronounce or order her to pronounce? And as for her family's wish to marry her off, it is not considered unless she herself seeks separation, except if she is foolish (safihah), then her guardian takes her place in case harm is confirmed for her.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage addresses the fifth issue: the ruling for a wife whose husband is missing and has left her without maintenance. The author clarifies that if she suffers extreme hardship or fears falling into sin, she may seek divorce immediately, without waiting for the four-year period that would apply if maintenance were still being paid. This is based on the opinion of Shaykh Khalil and his commentators. The procedure is that she must approach the qadi and prove lack of maintenance and her need. There is a difference of opinion whether the qadi himself pronounces the divorce or authorizes the wife to pronounce it. Additionally, the wife's family cannot force her to remarry against her will unless she is deemed legally incompetent (safihah), in which case her guardian may act in her best interest to prevent harm.

Key Terms
المفقود عنه (al-mafqud 'anha): The wife whose husband is missing
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance obligation of the husband towards his wife
التطليق بلا تأجيل (al-tatliq bi-la ta'jil): Divorce without delay, i.e., immediate right to seek divorce without waiting period

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition does the wife of a missing husband have the right to immediate divorce according to the passage?
A) Only if her husband has been missing for four years
B) If she has no maintenance or fears corruption, regardless of time
C) Only if the qadi permits it
D) Only if her family agrees

Key Takeaway
A wife whose missing husband fails to provide maintenance and she faces hardship or moral risk can obtain an immediate divorce by proving her situation to a qadi, who should authorize her to pronounce divorce.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Necessity of Qadi's Ruling for Wife's Self-Divorce
Explaining the necessity of qadi's involvement for wife's divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the necessity of establishing harm before a qadi to determine the validity of a wife's self-divorce and evaluate the enforceability of rulings from qadis appointed by non-Muslim authorities.

Core Idea
A wife cannot divorce herself without proving harm before a qadi; his ruling is essential.

In this passage, the author addresses the sixth issue – the legal procedure for a wife to obtain divorce when her husband is absent or fails to provide maintenance. The central ruling is that such a divorce is invalid unless the wife establishes her harm before a qadi. The passage also deals with the practical question of rulings from qadis appointed by non-Muslim rulers, a critical issue for Muslims living under non-Muslim governance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
State the question: Can a wife divorce herself if her husband is absent or fails to provide maintenance?
The passage explicitly says 'لا يحل' (it is not permissible) without prior qadi involvement.
2
Extract the Hanafi position
The wife must prove harm (thubut al-darar) before a qadi. The qadi must either pronounce divorce or order her to pronounce it.
This is based on earlier answers by Malik and Ibn Farhun's Tabsirah.
3
Examine the dalil
The hadith of Barirah: the Prophet gave her the choice to divorce herself. This supports the second opinion that the qadi authorizes the wife.
The author declares the second opinion stronger due to this hadith.
4
Apply to a modern scenario
For a Muslim woman under non-Muslim rule, she must approach a qadi (sharia council) even if appointed by non-Muslims, because his ruling is enforceable due to necessity.
Based on the fatwa of Imam al-Mazari.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واما المسئلة السادسه نجوا بها إنه لا يحل لمن لم تكن عندها نفقة او من خشيت الفساد من النساوان تطلق نفسها قبل ثبوت ضررها عند الحاكم سواء عدم النفقة وخشيت المضاد لما تقدم في الجواب عن المسئلة الأولى من جواب مالك وما تقدم في الجواب عن المسئلة. الثانية وهو قول قاضى المدنية ابن فرحون في تبصرته ان التطليق على الغائبين وغيرهم مما يفتقر إلى حكم الحاكم فلا بد من ثبوت متبررها عند الحاكم ناما ان يطلق الحاكم واما ان يامرها بتطليق نفسها وهو قولون مشهوران لكن القول الثاني اقوى لقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم البريرة لما عتقت انت املك بنفسك ان شئت اقمت مع زوجك وان شدت فارقتيه وأما قولكم وعلى الثاني كيف يعمل فالجواب عنها ان احكام قضاتهم نافذة مافية وان كانت توليتهم الصادرة من الكفار باطلة وبهذا اقتى الامام ابو عبد الله المارزى لما سئل عن احكامتاتي في زمنه من صقيلة من عند قاضيها او شهود عدل ها نا جاب جوابا طويلا الى ان قال واما الوجه الثاني وهو تولية الكافر
And as for the sixth issue, its answer is: It is not permissible for a woman who does not have maintenance or who fears corruption to divorce herself before establishing her harm before the qadi, whether it is absence of maintenance or fear of [؟]. This is based on what preceded in the answer to the first issue from the answer of Malik, and what preceded in the answer to the second issue, which is the statement of the qadi of Medina, Ibn Farhun, in his Tabsirah: 'Indeed, divorce upon absent persons and others requires the ruling of the qadi.' So it is necessary to establish her harm before the qadi. Either the qadi pronounces divorce or he orders her to divorce herself. These are two famous opinions, but the second opinion is stronger due to the statement of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) to Barirah when she was freed: 'You have authority over yourself; if you wish, you may stay with your husband, and if you wish, you may separate from him.' And as for your saying: 'How does it work according to the second opinion?' The answer: Indeed, the rulings of their qadis are enforceable, even if their appointment by the disbelievers is invalid. And Imam Abu Abdullah al-Mazari gave a fatwa on this when he was asked about rulings that came from Sicily from their qadi or just witnesses, and he gave a long answer until he said: 'As for the second aspect, it is the appointment of the disbeliever.'
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage addresses the sixth issue: the necessity of a qadi's involvement when a wife seeks divorce due to her husband's absence or lack of maintenance. The author rules that it is impermissible for a woman to pronounce divorce on herself without first proving her harm before a qadi. This is based on the opinions of Imam Malik and Qadi Ibn Farhun. The divorce of absent persons requires a qadi's ruling. There are two opinions: either the qadi pronounces the divorce himself or he authorizes the wife to pronounce it. The author declares the second opinion stronger, citing the hadith of Barirah where the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave her the choice to divorce herself. The passage also addresses a practical concern: the validity of rulings by qadis appointed by non-Muslim authorities. Imam al-Mazari held that such rulings are enforceable even if the appointment itself is invalid, due to necessity. This is a crucial principle for Muslims living under non-Muslim rule.

Key Terms
ثبوت الضرر (thubut al-darar): Establishment of harm before the qadi as a precondition for divorce
حكم الحاكم (hukm al-hakim): The ruling of the qadi that is necessary for the validity of the divorce
تولية الكافر (tawliyat al-kafir): Appointment of a non-Muslim as a judge or authority

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required before a wife can obtain a divorce from her absent husband?
A) She must wait four years
B) She must establish her harm before a qadi
C) She must have her family's permission
D) She must find a witness

Key Takeaway
A wife cannot divorce herself without establishing harm before a qadi; the qadi must either pronounce or authorize the divorce.
